In each of the past five years the results of crash tests have shown that car manufacturers are not taking sufficient steps to incorporate design features which would improve pedestrian protection in collisions with cars. Legislation to force manufacturers to make the necessary modifications is supposedly in the pipeline but has been repeatedly delayed. Given that around 8000 pedestrians are killed each year in Europe, with particularly high numbers in rural districts with poor roads, can the Presidency give an assurance that the rapid introduction of this vital legislation be given a high priority, and will the Presidency outline the timetable envisaged for the implementation process?

Answer

The Council has not received any Commission proposal for legislation compelling car manufacturers to incorporate design features which would improve pedestrian safety in the event of a collision between a car and a pedestrian. Without a Commission proposal the Council cannot even begin to give thought to a timetable for the adoption and implementation of such legislation.

Question No 27 by Olivier Dupuis (H−0278/99)

Subject: Tunisia: the case of Mr Khémaïs Ksila

Mr Khémaïs Ksila, vice‐chairman of the Tunisian League for the Protection of Human Rights, has been imprisoned for almost two years for his activities as a human rights militant. His situation is a cause for concern as he is a diabetic and has a heart condition. He was prosecuted on charges of ‘defamation against public policy and the public authorities', 'spreading false reports likely to cause a breach of the peace' and ‘incitement of citizens to break the law of the land' and sentenced, under scandalous circumstances, to 3 years' imprisonment and a heavy fine by the Tunis Criminal Court on 11 February 1998. This sentence was upheld on appeal on 25 April 1998 and his appeal on a point of law was hastily dismissed on 28 May 1998. In the unanimous opinion of his Tunisian lawyers and 9 international observers, Mr Khémaïs Ksila is the victim of a genuine miscarriage of justice made worse by the way in which the authorities have hounded him and his family. What steps has the Council taken since Mr Ksila's arrest to persuade the Tunisian authorities to release him, stop all forms of harassment of him and his family and as well as anyone militating for the establishment of democracy and the rule of law in Tunisia and for the observance of fundamental rights? Since any steps which may have been taken have been fruitless, what further steps does the Council intend to take to obtain the rapid release of Mr Ksila? Finally, does the Council not believe that in the light of such conduct on the part of the Tunisian State it would be advisable to review relations between the EU and Tunisia?

Answer

The Council is aware of the circumstances of the Khémaïs Ksila case referred to by the honourable Member.

The Council places great political importance on the spread and observance of human rights in Tunisia and is therefore paying close attention to developments there.

Tunisia was the first of the Mediterranean partners to conclude a EURO‐MED association agreement with the European Union, which established a direct political dialogue in which both sides can openly discuss political issues, including human rights.

The Council has, in various approaches, repeatedly raised the issue of human rights and fundamental freedoms with Tunisia and it will continue to do so in future, including in relation to the Khémaïs Ksila case.

Furthermore, human rights in Tunisia will be discussed in the course of the next meeting of the CFSP working group in May.

Question No 28 by Manuel Medina Ortega (H−0279/99)

Subject: Fishing agreement with Argentina

What is the Council's policy with regard to the present fishing agreement with the Argentine Republic, the Argentine Government's failures to comply with it and the prospects for renegotiation?

Answer

The Council is aware of the problems that have arisen as a result of the Argentine Republic"s new fisheries regulation. To solve these problems a meeting of the EC‐Argentina Joint Committee was held in Buenos Aires on 4/5 March 1999.

At the Joint Committee meeting the Community called upon the Argentine Government to comply with all the provisions of the fishing agreement with the European Community. It urged in particular that the discriminatory elements of the fisheries regulation (zoning of the hake fishing area) be withdrawn. The Community also expressed its regret that the agreement is no longer a balanced one as a result of the discriminatory legislation.

The negotiations on a new agreement are the responsibility of the Commission, which has already held exploratory talks with the Argentine Government on this issue. Following the conclusion of these exploratory talks the Council will establish an appropriate mandate for new negotiations in due course.

Question No 29 by John McCartin (H−0281/99)

Subject: Amnesty International claims concerning human rights in Egypt

Is the Council aware of Amnesty International claims regarding a worsening human rights situation in Egypt, particularly in respect of the numbers of deaths and the torture of prisoners in custody? Has the Council raised in any of its contacts with the Egyptian authorities the situation surrounding the death in 1996 of Karimi Nduthu, the Secretary‐General of the Release Political Prisoners Group, into which no inquest has yet been held?

Answer

The Council is aware of the reports by Amnesty International on human rights in Egypt. The EU has repeatedly taken up human rights issues with the Egyptian Government and it will maintain its commitment in future. The EU has not yet had an opportunity of taking up with the Egyptian Government the particular case you mention.

Human rights issues are also an important aspect of the negotiations currently under way on an association agreement between the EU and Egypt, since Egypt is not prepared to accept unreservedly the human rights clause required by the EU. The EU is therefore closely monitoring human rights in Egypt and is engaged in a dialogue with the Egyptian Government on these issues.

Question No 30 by Anna Karamanou (H−0284/99)

Subject: Organisation of impartial investigations into the disappearance of 3000 people in Algeria

According to Amnesty International, the problem of disappearances in Algeria has been shown to have intensified following revelations of arbitrary arrests and illegal detention. More particularly, during the last six years, about 3000 men and women have disappeared and only very few of them have been traced following prolonged period of secret detention. The authorities and the security services are refusing to provide any information concerning the fate of detainees to their families, who are desperately searching not only hospitals and army camps but also mortuaries and graveyards for even the slightest clue as to their whereabouts. What steps will be Council take to launch a full, impartial and independent inquiry into all these disappearances and to ensure effective protection for human rights in Algeria?

Answer

The human rights situation in Algeria has improved significantly in the last year. This is also clear from reports by independent human rights organisations.

The disappearances date mostly from the mid−1990s; recently there have been hardly any such cases.

The Algerian Government is showing an obvious willingness to cooperate in this area and has now contacted the ICRC in order to deal with the subject of disappearances.

The Algerian foreign minister, Mr Attaf, has already agreed, during the EU‐Algeria consultations in October 1998 under the Austrian Presidency, to examine the cases raised by the European Union.

This topic has been dealt with on several occasions in the CFSP working group responsible. The presidency last presented Algeria with documented individual cases for examination in March. The dialogue with the Algerian Government and independent human rights organisations will be continued by the Council, including the Heads of Missions in Algiers.

Question No 31 by Mark Killilea (H−0290/99)

Subject: Monitoring of equal opportunities across the Structural Funds

The European Employment Strategy, adopted at the Luxembourg Summit, is committed to creating labour markets that provide equal opportunities and freedom from discrimination. Can the Council indicate what specific mechanisms will be put in place to ensure adequate implementation of such measures and how monitoring of Member States' activities in this respect can be carried out?

Answer

The Employment Policy Guidelines adopted in 1998 following the European Council of Luxembourg contain equal opportunities as one of the four pillars of the new employment strategy.

In the light of experience of the first year of transposition of the directives, significant importance is attached to the equal opportunities aspect of the 1999 Employment Guidelines (OJ C 69, 12.3.1999, p. 2). Directive No 19 extends equal opportunities so that it also covers the other three pillars of the strategy (employability, entrepreneurship and adaptability).

The details of transposition of the Employment Policy Guidelines are left to the discretion of the Member States. They can present, in the National Action Plans to be submitted in June 1999, those measures with which they are endeavouring to achieve the objectives set out in the Guidelines.

To ensure appropriate monitoring of transposition of the Guidelines, the introduction to the text of the 1999 Guidelines stresses the need for common indicators which must be drawn up at Community level and on the basis of comparable statistics. The Committee on Employment and the Labour Market, with the technical support of the Commission's services, is actively engaged in identifying such indicators.

Question No 32 by Alan Gillis (H−0292/99)

Subject: Angola

Given that Angola has now slipped back into civil war and the actions of both sides in this conflict are having a disastrous effect on the people of Angola in areas controlled both by the Governing MPLA and UNITA, it is the ordinary people yet again who are paying the price. What steps can the Council take to bring pressure on the warring sides to come to the conciliation table and therefore bring this suffering to an end?

Answer

The Council fully shares the honourable Member's deep concern at the current situation in Angola. We have made known our position on Angola on numerous occasions and we have repeatedly called on both parties – the government and UNITA – to honour their obligations under the Lusaka Protocol. We have repeatedly called on the government not to seek a military solution.

In its Resolution 1229 of 26 February 1999 on extending the mandate of MONUA, the UN observer mission, the UN's Security Council made it clear that UNITA bears the main responsibility for the present situation because of its failure to honour the Lusaka Protocol of 1994.

The Lusaka Protocol remains the only legal framework for a peaceful negotiated solution to the conflict. The Council is of the opinion that the EU should continue the policy it has pursued hitherto of supporting the efforts at conciliation by leading politicians from the UN and from the region and of ensuring effective implementation of the UN sanctions in Angola.

The Council is all too aware that it is the ordinary people of Angola who have to pay the price of this conflict. The EU is making efforts, both via ECHO and bilaterally on the part of the Member States, to ease the suffering through humanitarian aid. However, the only possibility of a solution to the conflict lies in an end to the fighting and in UNITA pursuing the path of peaceful negotiations.

Question No 33 by María Izquierdo Rojo (H−0294/99)

Subject: Reduction in European funding for Spain

The amount of Structural Funds and Cohesion Funds that Spain will receive for the period 2000‐2006 was agreed at the Berlin Council. Compared with the upward trend of the period 1993‐1999, is it not the case that Spain's cohesion funding is decreasing? Is it not also the case that the amount agreed for the next round of funding is lower than the Commission's most pessimistic predictions and the worse‐case scenarios discussed in the European Parliament?

Answer

At its special summit in Berlin on 24/25 March, the European Council decided that the Cohesion Fund, whose fundamental objectives continue to be valid, should also be available to Member States in the euro zone whose national level of prosperity is less than 90 % of EU GNP. In order to take account of the increase in prosperity of euro participants as a result of previous support measures, the total appropriations were adjusted accordingly. The award of EUR 18 billion agreed in Berlin is, in the Council"s view, clear proof of the high priority given to cohesion policy and solidarity in the European Union. It is also welcome news in this context that the issue of the indicative allocation of Cohesion Fund resources to recipient states has now been successfully resolved in the Council.

Question No 34 by James Fitzsimons (H−0295/99)

Subject: Fighting drugs

According to the EMCDDA 1998 Annual report on the state of the drugs problem in the European Union, the total figure for internal spending in 1997 on EU drug‐related actions was ECU 33.3 million.

What assurances can the Council give that, in the light of its decisions in Berlin on EU expenditure over the coming years, future expenditure on drug‐prevention programmes and actions will not be cut back but will, in fact, be stepped up on the basis of an increase in appropriations starting in the financial year 2000?

Answer

In answer to the honourable Member"s question, it should be pointed out that the Council attaches the highest importance to the fight against drugs whose harmful effects are undermining the social fabric of our democracies.

The Commission has not yet submitted its draft general budget for the year 2000, and so the Council is not really in a position to comment on the amount of appropriations earmarked for this important aspect of Community policies. However, the Council can assure the honourable Member that it will give this matter its full attention.

Question No 35 by Niall Andrews (H−0297/99)

Subject: Involvement of all ‘stakeholders' in the development of EU‐supported Urban strategies and initiatives in cities such as Dublin

The Commission has submitted to the Council and the European Parliament an important Communication on ‘Sustainable Urban Development in the European Union: a Framework for Action' in which it argues that all stakeholders, including citizens, should be involved in the formulation of integrated urban strategies for sustainable development. It also argues that EU policies need to be made more ‘urban sensitive'.

With 80 % of the EU population living in towns and cities, and with urban areas having to bear the brunt of economic, social and transport problems, will the Council give an assurance that the Urban Initiative, supported from the current Structural Funds, will be central to future policies aimed at developing successful urban strategies, a matter which is vital for a rapidly expanding city such as Dublin?

Answer

The Council is aware of the specific problems of urban areas and it has always attached particular importance to strategies for successful town planning.

In the context of the talks on Agenda 2000, the European Council in Berlin also reached political agreement on reform of the EU's structural funds.

Accordingly, the European Council has adopted the Commission's proposal to reduce the number of Community initiatives to three: Interreg, EQUAL and Leader. The objectives of the Community initiatives which are to be discontinued – including URBAN – can be pursued under the new Objectives 1, 2 and 3.

Furthermore, urban problem areas (accounting for 2 % of the EU's population) will be eligible for particular support in the new Objective 2. These areas include those with a high population density which are suffering from above‐average unemployment, high levels of poverty, environmental damage and crime, etc.

Subject to the legislative procedure with Parliament, it is the Council's view that the future structural aid period will assign high priority to urban development policy, and that it will therefore allay the honourable Member's concerns.

Question No 36 by Pat Gallagher (H−0299/99)

Subject: Outcome of the Berlin Summit and the common fisheries policy

In the light of the conclusions of the Berlin Summit, will the Council indicate the likely consequences for the common fisheries policy and the development of the fishing and fish‐processing industries in Ireland?

Answer

The general compromise reached at the Berlin Summit entails including the fisheries sector in Objective 1 support and horizontal support outside Objective 1 regions.

Measures outside the Objective 1 regions will be supported by the Financial Instrument for Fisheries Guidance (FIFG) which has funds of EUR 1.1 billion.

The criteria for support are set out in Article 2 of the draft of the FIFG in conformity with the Commission proposal for an implementing regulation. This proposal is currently under discussion in the Fisheries Council which is expected to take a decision on 10 June,

Since the practical details of aid are set out in the implementing regulation, it is not possible at present to give a precise assessment of the impact on the development of fisheries and the fish‐processing industry in Ireland. The actual effects will also depend on what measures the Irish authorities focus on in the programming procedure.

I welcome this opportunity of being able to refer to the great success of structural aid for Ireland and the fact that Ireland's per capita GDP is approaching the Community mean. The proposal provides for a general structural fund regulation with a six‐year transitional period for regions which will no longer be eligible for Objective 1 aid.

Question No 37 by Liam Hyland (H−0301/99)

Subject: Development of the Irish and European food industry

The food industry plays a vital role in the Irish and other European economies. In the face of a highly competitive international market and the additional need to ensure top priority for food safety issues, will the Council state what new initiatives could be taken at EU level to benefit Irish and European food producers and enable them to strengthen their position on home and world markets?

Answer

Food safety is a matter to which the Council attaches considerable importance, particularly in relation to protection of the health of consumers but also in the light of the European food industry. All legislation concerning food is especially concerned with safety and quality. The European Council of Luxembourg gave expression to this in its statement on food safety.

It is, in fact, the Commission's task to submit to the Council proposals for initiatives in this area. No such proposal is currently before the Council.

The statement by the European Council of Luxembourg is contained in the presidency conclusions of 12 and 13 December 1997 (SN 400/97, p. 37). In the statement, the European Council stresses that ‘the production and supply of safe food must be one of the European Union's priorities' and that ‘a high level of health protection' must be guaranteed.

With regard to BSE, the Commission submits a report of activities to the Council and the European Parliament twice a year.

Question No 38 by Karin Riis‐Jørgensen (H−0305/99)

Subject: Risks connected with BSE

One of the conclusions of the BSE conference of 30 November and 1 December 1998 was that it is not possible to operate with a no‐risk concept in food safety. Can the Council state what scientific risks there are in connection with BSE, e.g. what is the risk level compared with that of other forms of risks in food?

Furthermore, is it possible to provide copies of the reports covering the observations made during the visits by members of the SSC (scientific steering committee) to the meat producers' factories – the dates of the visits, the names of the SSC members and their conclusions from the visits?

Answer

Under the heading ‘Lessons to be learned from the BSE crisis", the second joint European Commission and European Parliament conference held on 30 November and 1 December 1998 made an interim assessment of work done so far to combat mad‐cow disease. Around 450 politicians and scientists, civil servants and industrialists, consumers and farmers spent two days discussing future strategies for food safety.

The Council also welcomes the fact that consumers and society in general can be informed about existing risks and that an open dialogue can be held on the subject. The Treaty of Amsterdam actually now enshrines the consumer"s right to information and provides a further boost to this policy of openness.

However, questions concerning the scientific evaluation of risks in food should really be addressed not to the Council but to the European Commission, which can also provide information on the composition of the Scientific Steering Committee and on the reports and conclusions produced by these experts.

Question No 39 by Gary Titley (H−0308/99)

Subject: POWs still missing following the Gulf War

Eight years after the end of the Gulf War, the fate of over 600 POWs (mainly Kuwaitis) remains unknown.

Under the terms of UN Resolution 687, Iraq is obligated to cooperate with the International Committee of the Red Cross and account for and release all POWs. In December 1998, however, Iraq stopped collaborating with the ICRC.

Will the Council now bring pressure to bear on Iraq to account fully for all POWs it took during the Gulf War and release immediately and unconditionally any POWs it still holds?

Answer

It is barbaric that eight years after the end of the Gulf War there are still prisoners of war and civilian detainees and that no information is available on their fate.

This is an item which will remain on the agenda of the international community until it has been resolved.

Implementation of Security Council Resolution 687 (the cease‐fire resolution of 1991) is, in the first instance, the responsibility of the Security Council itself which acts on behalf of all the UN's member states. In return, the member states implement the measures mandated by the Security Council: in this case, the sanctions.

The question of POWs is one which is on the agenda of the Security Council. The third of the panels set up on 30 January has just published its report, which is largely devoted to this question. The report will be one of the bases for the Security Council's future stance towards Iraq. As stated above, the issue will remain on the agenda until it is resolved.

At the same time, the honourable Member will be aware of how difficult if is to exert pressure on the Iraqi leadership with any prospect of success – and if it is difficult for the Security Council it is even more so for the Council.

Against this background, the Council respects the approach of the ICRC, which treats the fate of the POWs and – to use the ICRC's wording – 'persons whose whereabouts is not known' as a humanitarian and not a political problem, and which conducts its negotiations in the utmost confidentiality, and once again it calls on Iraq to resume cooperation within the Tripartite Commission.

Question No 42 by Anneli Hulthén (H−0315/99)

Subject: Azo food dyes and the internal market

In Sweden we have hitherto had a ban on azo food dyes, which is now being lifted in the interests of the functioning of the internal market. These are a group of synthetic dyes which are used among other things to colour desserts, as well as sweets and ice‐cream, which of course appeal in particular to children.

Does the German Presidency consider that we can guarantee the safety of our citizens when we know that these substances may be allergenic, and can we in the European Union really accept our children being needlessly exposed to dangerous food dyes?

Answer

The lifting of the ban on azo food dyes in Sweden is based on a Commission decision that was published in Official Journal L 3 of 7 January 1999, page 13. It would therefore be up to the Commission to explain this decision to the honourable Member.

The Council would merely like to point out that European Parliament and Council Directive 94/36/EC on colours for use in foodstuffs – which also lays down conditions for the use of azo food dyes – is based on an opinion delivered by the Scientific Committee for Food assessing the safety of additives.

Question No 43 by Christine Oddy (H−0316/99)

Subject: European Company Statute

What progress is being made by the German Presidency in discussions on the European Company Statute?

Answer

There is considerable agreement between the Member States on the substance of the draft regulation on the European Company Statute. The main stumbling‐block to its adoption is the lack of agreement on the directive on participation by workers in European companies, which as far as the Member States are concerned is a prerequisite for adoption of the regulation.

Following a failure to reach agreement on the abovementioned directive at the Labour and Social Affairs Council of 2 December 1998, the presidency is currently endeavouring to find a solution through bilateral talks with a number of Member States. The next specialist Councils (for the directive, the Labour and Social Affairs Council on 25 May 1999; for the regulation, the Internal Market Council on 21 June 1999) will be looking at this question again.

I can assured the honourable Member that the presidency attaches the highest priority to a successful conclusion of the matter of the European company.

Question No 44 by Carlos Bru Purón (H−0319/99)

Subject: Introduction of a European eco‐tax

The Federal Republic of Germany has decided to levy a tax on the use of hydrocarbons, varying from 0.2 DM per kw/h to between 0.06 and 0.32 DM per kw/hour, according to circumstances.

Is the Council not of the opinion that the time has now come to introduce this eco‐tax, or a similar one, throughout Europe?

Answer

Germany did indeed introduce an environmental tax reform on 1 April 1999, and the products which are being taxed are diesel, petrol, domestic fuel oil, petroleum gas and electricity. The tax revenue is returned in full to the taxpayers by means of reductions of 0.8 % in pension insurance contributions. This has benefits for the environment and also promotes employment, thus representing a double dividend.

Other EU Member States such as Denmark, Sweden and the Netherlands have been busy introducing eco‐taxes as part of environmental tax reforms for several years now, and other states such as Austria and Belgium have environmental components in their tax systems. Since the beginning of this year Italy has introduced the first of five phases of an environmental tax reform, and is the first southern state to do so. The United Kingdom has also announced its intention to introduce a tax on industrial energy consumption in 2001.

So more and more Member States are introducing environmental tax reforms. For competition reasons energy‐intensive industries generally come under a special taxation scheme, but this could be reduced if all states introduced this type of energy taxation. So in order to help to harmonise the internal market the European Commission presented on 12 March 1997 a ‘Proposal for a Council Directive restructuring the Community framework for the taxation of energy products". Consequently, EU‐wide minimum rates of taxation, which already exist today for mineral oil products, are also to be introduced for all other energy products and, like those for mineral oil products, are to be increased in two further phases.

Discussions on this proposal have been underway since 1997. At the end of 1998 the European Council in Vienna asked the Council to continue to work on a Community framework for energy taxation. At the same time it pointed to the promises of the Luxembourg and Cardiff Councils to integrate the environment and sustainable development into all Community policies in line with the Treaty of Amsterdam.

The Ecofin Council is currently discussing the Commission proposal of March 1997 to further harmonise the taxation of energy products. The German Presidency has focused on this issue and set itself the goal of achieving a basic consensus between all Member States on the need to harmonise energy products and on the structure of such harmonisation. This should open the way to practical negotiations on the details of the Commission proposal.

Any form of support from the other Member States and the European Parliament for the harmonisation of energy taxation is therefore very welcome, especially as a majority of states are in favour of it. The objective is now to convince the states which do not agree in principle of the need for and benefits of this proposal.

2. Questions to the Commission

 

Question No 45 by Alexandros Alavanos (H−0225/99)

Subject: Abolition of the ‘bonus‐malus' factor in compulsory motor vehicle insurance

It has been reported in the press that the option of transferring the deduction in car insurance premiums under the ‘bonus‐malus' system is to be abolished. In view of the fact that some 70 % of Greek policy‐holders who have a positive ‘bonus‐malus' balance are affected and that the proposed abolition of the transfer of the deduction as it applies today will undermine competition, given that consumers will be reluctant to take out an insurance policy with a different insurance company offering lower rates, since they would thereby lose the deduction in their insurance premium, will the Commission say whether such an arrangement is in keeping with the European Union's rules on competition?

Answer

The abolition of the ‘bonus‐malus' system in Greece, to which the honourable Member refers, could be beneficial for both insurers and consumers.

Since the abolition of the former system in 1996, each insurer has been free to set his own premiums and develop his own customer policy. Greece abolished the ‘bonus‐malus' system when transposing Directive 92/49/EEC (the third directive on non‐life insurance) which provides for freedom of rates and the removal of prior checks on premiums and contracts by the supervisory authorities of the Member State of origin of the insurer. More specifically, the Greek authorities have eliminated the provision laying down a compulsory ‘bonus‐malus' system, on the grounds that such a system was a price‐related measure and therefore contravened the third directive on non‐life insurance.

As for consumers, although it is true that a uniform ‘bonus‐malus' system makes it easier to transfer from one insurer to another, the freedom to set premiums has other advantages for consumers: Greek insurers will be free to take account of the ‘bonus' accrued with another insurer under the old system and, indeed, to grant even larger deductions than those allowable within the narrow bounds of that system. Moreover, depending on the background of each new client, the new insurer may vary the premium on the basis of several criteria, unlike what happened before. The variation in the criteria taken into account and the individual assessment of the risk posed by each policy‐holder may lead to greater competition on the market and to a reduction in premiums, to the benefit of consumers seeking third‐party motor vehicle insurance on the Greek market. All insurers are free to make an offer, and their customers are free to make up their own minds.

In any event, the Commission remains competent under Community law to handle any further questions in this sector.

Question No 46 by Paul Rübig (H−0226/99)

Subject: Raising public procurement thresholds

In its resolution of 9 February 1999 the European Parliament called for the threshold values for public service, supply and works contracts to be raised.

Would the Commission expect such action to ease the burden on companies tendering for public contracts, for example for SMEs and in respect of the paperwork required, or to remove existing barriers in the internal market?

Answer

Threshold values for the application of the public procurement measures have been agreed so as to cover procurement potentially tradeable across frontiers.

The directives are thus meant to align the procedures to be followed in each Member State whenever a contract is to be awarded with a value exceeding, the thresholds, whereas the general principles enshrined in the Treaty apply to all contracts irrespective of their value.

The Commission is aware that the application of the Community directives(2) may sometimes result in a heavy administrative burden, particularly for SMEs. This message came out clearly from some citizens and some representative organisations, which were recently consulted. It is also correct that the inter‐market penetration of public procurement is not as high as it is in other economic sectors.

The Commission is committed to cooperate with Member States and interested parties to improve the situation. However, the Commission is of the opinion that the answer to a sometimes disproportionate administrative burden does not lie in eliminating common procedures, including the right to remedies, which are necessary to ensure clarity and competition in public procurement. Rather, the answer lies in making the procedures simpler, more understandable and less costly.

This is why the Commission has started several actions as discussed in Parliament on the occasion of the adoption of the Tappin report on the Communication of the Commission on Public Procurement in the European Union on 9 February 1999:

1. Firstly, the Commission is preparing some amendments to the existing legislation in order to simplify it whenever possible and to introduce flexibility. Those amendments will be presented to the Parliament and the Council in the next few months.
2. Secondly, the Commission is issuing interpretative documents in order to clarify the existing rules, in particular those which are coming more and more into use: for example a draft communication on the rules applicable to concessions has been put out for consultation.
3. Thirdly, the Commission is taking measures to promote electronic procurement. For example, the SIMAP (Système d'Information pour les Marchés Publics) website, which is free of charge from 1 January 1999, is an initiative aiming at improving the quality of information for SMEs about procurement opportunities and ensuring that information is available to all potentially interested suppliers.

Those measures should reduce administrative barriers for enterprises wishing to take part in tendering procedures, whether national or throughout the Community.

In any case, the Commission will continue to follow closely this matter to ensure that the measures taken actually produce the expected results.

Question No 47 by Ana Palacio Vallelersundi (H−0234/99)

Subject: Greek law on television advertisements targeted at minors

The proceedings commenced under Article 169 of the EC Treaty with regard to the Greek law banning the televising of advertisements for children's toys between 7 a.m. and 10 p.m. have been under consideration for a number of years now, and the Commission has already forwarded a letter of formal notice, arguing that the measure in question is excessive. In view of this situation, can the Commission say what steps it has taken to bring the aforesaid proceedings to a conclusion, forward the studies, reports and other documents drawn up on this matter, and compare the approach in each of the Member States to the advertising of toys on television?

Answer

In order to clarify and facilitate its assessment of the proportionality of the Greek law, at the end of 1998 the Commission collected a number of recent studies on the subject of advertising and children, which examined from a scientific point of view the dangers to children of being exposed to televised advertising, mainly for toys. The Commission services have drawn up a document summarising the main results of this research. This document, which I will make available to the honourable Member, does not include any working conclusions because of the sometimes contradictory points of view expressed by the researchers. It nonetheless served as a basic consultation document for a group of experts on commercials which held a round table on the subject on 22 October 1998. During this interesting debate we saw once again the diversity of current opinions on this subject.

The results were broadly speaking as follows: Greece is the only Member State to almost totally prohibit television advertising for toys. Sweden prohibits all television advertising targeted at minors. More limited prohibitive measures are found in Ireland and in the Dutch‐speaking area of Belgium, where advertising is banned during and immediately before and after children"s television programmes. The other Member States have, on the whole, simply adopted the provisions contained in the ‘Television without Frontiers" directive(3) . The justifications put forward by the Member States who have introduced prohibitive measures are essentially that children are unable to comprehend the commercial purpose of advertising and to differentiate between a commercial and another programme.

In the light of the information gathered, the new Commission will have to decide what to do next. I firmly believe it is necessary to find a rapid solution to this dispute so as to ensure a balance between legitimate concerns for the protection of children and the constraints of the single market.

Moreover, in the course of this year, in line with its promises to Parliament and the Council, the Commission is to launch an extensive study on the general problem of the impact of television advertising and of tele‐shopping on minors, in the context of evaluating the implementation of the ‘Television without Frontiers" directive.

Question No 48 by Thomas Megahy (H−0251/99)

Subject: Spanish Gibraltar frontier

Restrictions by Spanish border guards at the Spanish/Gibraltar frontier are now causing delays of up to five hours or more. This is causing considerable difficulty on both sides of the frontier. The tourist industry in Gibraltar is badly hurt as are the 3000 people from La Línea legally working in Gibraltar who are protesting to their own government that the border restrictions threaten their livelihood. What steps does the Commission propose to re‐establish right of free movement for EU citizens?

Answer

The Commission has been informed from various sources about the difficulties mentioned by the honourable MEP encountered by European citizens when crossing the border at Gibraltar. They concern the border checks applied both to persons and to their belongings (vehicles, luggage in particular), which would entail according to these sources from 3 to 5 hours waiting time.

In particular, as far as the free movement of persons(4) is concerned, since one of the two interested Member States is not a member of the Schengen Treaty, it is not possible to apply the Schengen system that has abolished all border controls. At a border, like the one at Gibraltar, controls are allowed within the frame of current Community legislation. Pursuant to such provisions, Member States must allow citizens of the Union to enter their territory simply on production of valid identity card or passport, subject only to the limitations justified on grounds of public policy, public security or public health.

As a matter of principle the legality of national provisions is subject to the proportionality test. The Court of Justice attaches special importance to the principle that the action of public authorities, whether Community or national, and obligations imposed on citizens who exercise individual rights or freedoms guaranteed by the Treaty, should be proportionate to the legal objectives sought by public authorities(5) . Any Community or national measures limiting the exercise of a fundamental right such as the right to move freely within the territory of the Member States shall therefore respect the proportionality principle.

In view of the above, the Commission is in contact with the Spanish authorities, in order to assure that the measures applied are compatible with Community law. The Commission will keep the honourable MEP informed on the follow‐up.

Question No 49 by Helena Torres Marques (H−0275/99)

Subject: Disappointment of citizens in the euro zone regarding private use of the euro

Even though the countries of the euro zone have had a single currency since 1 January 1999, citizens of the zone wishing to use the euro are obliged to pay charges as though they were conducting transactions in more than one currency.

According to information provided by experts on the banking system, these charges may continue even beyond 2002.

The great advantage of the single currency rests on the significant savings which it was expected to offer in cross‐border payments and above all in certain sectors such as tourism.

In order to rectify this situation, there will need to be an inter‐bank payment system at internal‐ market level so that the euro zone can operate in the same way as the inter‐bank system in individual countries. Only then will the public see any advantage in using the euro rather than national currencies.

Can the Commission say when such a European system for payments in euros is to be set up and when the euro will be used and payments made in euros without entailing costs for citizens within the euro zone, thus bringing into being a genuine European internal market?

Answer

The question from the honourable Member concerns both the exchange of euro area national banknotes and cross‐border payments in euro in small amounts.

As regards the exchange of euro area national banknotes, the Commission is monitoring the situation closely. To this end, the Commission has asked the European banking associations to supply information about the past and present level of exchange fees. On 16 February, the Commission also announced an inquiry into whether banks have acted in breach of the competition rules in the Treaty, when setting their new exchange fees. It should be noted that the problem of fees for the exchange of euro area national banknotes is a transitional problem, which will be solved when euro banknotes are issued in 2002. Under Article 2, section (b) of Commission Recommendation 98/286/EC of 23.04.1998, the Commission expects banks to exchange household amounts of their national banknotes and coin into euro banknotes and coin, at least for their own customers, free of charge. The European banking associations have stated that their members will comply with the Commission Recommendation. It should also be noted that, during the transitional period, national central banks in the euro area are willing, subject to certain conditions, to exchange other euro area national banknotes into their own national banknotes free of charge.

As regards cross‐border euro payments in small amounts, the Commission has also asked the European banking associations to supply information about the past and present level of charges. The most important case relates to cross‐border transfers, where the cost of conversion for the banks represents only a small proportion of their total costs. Their other costs relate to working time, systems costs, telecommunications costs etc. As the honourable Member says, these other costs will not disappear, even in 2002. However, these costs could be reduced if the links between cross‐border retail payment systems were more efficient than they are at present. Some of the necessary elements for improving these links (e.g. the definition of the new ‘International Bank Account Number') are already in place. But more needs to be done to enable charges for small‐value cross‐border charges to be reduced towards the level of domestic charges.

With this in mind, the Commission has announced in the Framework for Action that it intends to map out a common strategy for mobilising the efforts of the public and private sector in order to achieve the goal of a Single Payments Area for retail payments. The Commission will come forward with this Communication as soon as circumstances allow. The Communication will examine the existing barriers to small‐value cross‐border payments and propose concrete measures in order to reduce them.

The Commission agrees that it is not acceptable in the single currency area for charges on small‐value cross‐border transfers to exceed charges on domestic transfers by several orders of magnitude.

Question No 50 by Francisca Sauquillo Pérez del Arco (H−0231/99)

Subject: Operation of the Commission's common external service (CES)

As a result of the reform of the Commission services, whereby the responsibilities of DG IB and DG VIII for development cooperation with non‐governmental organisations are being brought together within a common external service (CES), many applications for project co‐financing and approval are being held up.

Can the Commission say how many projects are being processed at present within the said service and the dates on which those still awaiting processing were submitted?

Question No 51 by Josep Pons Grau (H−0232/99)

Subject: Common external service

A number of European non‐governmental organisations have encountered problems in the processing of projects they have submitted to the CES for co‐financing, apparently because of staff shortages. How does the Commission intend to resolve the problem of staff shortages and speed up the processing of projects?

Question No 52 by Anna Terrón i Cusí (H−0233/99)

Subject: Common external service

In view of the fact that since the CES was set up the processing of many applications from non‐governmental organisations for Commission funding has been held up, does the Commission believe that it has the number of officials required for the CES to fulfil its remit?

Joint answer

At the outset, the Commission wishes to pay tribute to the work carried out by NGOs in Third World development and notably with disadvantaged groups of the population in some of the poorest countries.

The Commission is actively exploring means of streamlining procedures for the selection and management of the very large number of individual NGO projects benefiting from Community grants. The outcome of this work will go much of the way to addressing the concerns raised by the honourable Members.

The newly‐established Joint Relex Service plays a key role in this process as its remit includes the simplification and harmonisation of funding procedures in all areas of external policy, excluding humanitarian assistance where the Commission has already proceeded with a simplification of procedures by means of a specific instrument to this area.

The Commission decision establishing the Joint Relex Service, set up on 1 July 1998, foresaw a staffing level of 648 plus the Director General, six Directors and staff directly attached to them. However this level has not been reached, notably because of the reduction in the 1999 Budget for appropriations for external staff. The Commission is considering urgently how to deal with this shortfall in the staffing of the Joint Relex Service.

Question No 53 by Roberto Speciale (H−0237/99)

Subject: Trade in human beings in the Sudan

Recent reports in an Italian national daily newspaper have highlighted the existence of trade in human beings in Sudan, especially in the south of the country, including minors from the Dinka and Nuba tribes in particular.

Is the Commission aware of this situation?

If so, has it contacted the Sudanese authorities to verify these reports and express the European Union's total opposition to such practices?

What reprisals mights the European Community take in order to end this trade (beginning with the suspension of all aid and cooperation programmes)?

Answer

Following allegations in the international press about the existence of contemporary forms of slavery in Sudan, the Commission has been monitoring this issue very closely. Continuing human rights abuses take place in both civil war and civil rights contexts. The Commission is very concerned about the impact of the current conflict in Sudan on the situation of human rights and its adverse effect on the civilian population.

Because of the lack of respect of human rights and human rights violations, the Commission suspended its development cooperation with Sudan in 1990 and development assistance was phased out. This position continues in 1999 reflecting the concerns of the Union in respect of human rights violations, the lack of moves towards democracy and the armed conflict in the south.

The Commission continues to follow the situation in Sudan very carefully and avails itself of every opportunity to urge the Sudanese government to allow unimpeded access for monitors and international observers and to investigate allegations on abduction promptly, publish their findings and punish offenders.

Question No 54 by Clive Needle (H−0289/99)

Subject: Outstanding payments

Amongst numerous complaints concerning outstanding payments from the Commission to organisations carrying out valuable work in the field of development, a company based in the East of England has now been forced to close because the Commission has not made the relevant payment.

Could the Commission explain what progress has been made in improving the efficiency and speed of payment and what assurances are being given to companies and organisations legitimately seeking to contribute to programmes supported by Parliament?

Answer

The Commission very much regrets the delay which occurred in making a payment to the company in the honourable Member's constituency, a delay which was exacerbated by the fact that the payment was made to the incorrect account after the beneficiary had changed their bank.

More generally, the Commission has taken steps to speed up payments and has set its services a deadline of 60 days from the receipt of a valid payment claim to the transfer of the payment from the Commission bank account. Nonetheless, this deadline is still exceeded in too many cases.

In order to improve the management of external aid, including in the area of development, the Commission established a new directorate‐general in 1998, the Joint Relex Service. One of the tasks of the SCR is to simplify procedures in order to make the handling of payment requests more straightforward.

This will be done, inter alia, by the simplification and harmonisation of procurement procedures and the standardisation of contracts. A clear and consistent definition of the documentation required from a beneficiary for a payment claim to be processed will assist both the claimant and the Commission services.

Question No 55 by Nelly Maes (H−0304/99)

Subject: Use made of aid to countries at war in the Great Lakes region

The numerous wars currently in progress in Africa are kept going by a constant supply of arms. The Council considers that the Union should adopt a general approach to aid to countries at war. In particular, it should be ascertained whether European tax‐payers' money is being used to finance wars.

Has the Commission completed its inquiry into the use made of aid from Member States to countries at war? Have the findings been announced?

Aid from the European budget to such warring countries as Rwanda and Uganda is indirectly equivalent to aid for arms procurement. Under the European structural adjustment programme for Rwanda, EUR 24.6 m is to be provided in budgetary aid.

How can it be maintained that European tax‐payers' money is not being used for arms procurement when for example Uganda's defence expenditure has risen by 27 % and when Rwanda is capable of occupying parts of Congo with a force of 16 000 soldiers who cost $180 per day?

Answer

Armed conflicts cause immense human suffering, destroy the efforts of human and economic development and undermine the solidarity among ACP countries, which is the basis for the Lomé partnership.

In recent times vast regions of Africa have become war zones, others hosting millions of refugees and displaced people – 4 million in southern Sudan, almost 3 million in the Great Lakes region, several hundred thousand in Angola and other neighbouring countries of Congo – and about a dozen governments are spending scarce resources to buy arms and to sustain military efforts.

Exceptional situations require exceptional measures. In parallel to the review of the institutional framework in the post Lomé negotiations, the Community has to undertake a profound review of its relationship with Africa from the point of view of conflict management and conflict containment, in addition to the efforts we already undertake in conflict prevention. The objectives of trade liberalisation and economic reform to facilitate Africa's integration into the global economy will become meaningless if substantial parts of the continent sink into chaos and protracted armed conflict.

In view of the close relationship established by the Lomé Convention, the Community has a special responsibility to assist the ACP countries in finding peaceful solutions to conflicts among them, but also the duty to ensure that any funds made available for ACP countries are used in accordance with the provisions of the Convention and with the foreign policy objectives enshrined in the treaties of the European Union, and in particular to prevent a diversion of funds for belligerent purposes.

A debate with the Member States of the Community on this issue has been launched and the Commission welcomes the honourable Member's question which brings the issue to the floor of Parliament.

Certain measures have already been taken by the Commission in order to improve the existing control mechanisms: for all new programmes of direct budgetary assistance (notably structural adjustment support, STABEX and SYSMIN), the Commission introduces clauses for payment in instalments and periodic reviews of the financing agreement requesting full budgetary transparency from the beneficiary government. These evaluations are carried out in close cooperation with the international finance institutions. Furthermore a letter to the Head of Government of the beneficiary country expresses the Commission's concern about the involvement in armed conflict, the hope for an early withdrawal and for a constructive attitude of the government concerned in the search for a peaceful solution.

It is clear, however, that the response to conflict situations of the scope we are witnessing today in Africa cannot be limited to these steps taken by the Commission. The Community has to consider what measures can be taken to curb arms trade, to implement sanctions imposed by UN Security Council, to promote negotiations etc. But it has also to consider whether it is appropriate to continue with the present levels of development assistance to countries which are spending a considerable amount of their resources for military purposes that can hardly be justified as ‘legitimate self defence'.

Although the policy decision will have to be taken on a case‐by‐case basis, it is essential to make sure that measures are not taken in an isolated manner, but as part of a coherent strategy with clear and transparent objectives.

Question No 56 by Concepció Ferrer (H−0307/99)

Subject: EU/South Africa trade agreement

In connection with the free trade negotiations between the Union and South Africa, certain industrial products, particularly those in the textile and apparel sectors, have been affected by factors such as the asymmetrical dismantling of tariffs and the possibility of a much wider definition of mixed origin than in other agreements. Will the Commission explain what measures it is currently taking, or intends to take, to guarantee that the opening of markets is a two‐way process?

Answer

The Commission has negotiated a trade, development and cooperation agreement with the Republic of South Africa in accordance with the negotiating directives it received from the Council in June 1995 and March 1996. According to these last directives, the negotiations, in the trade field, were aimed at establishing a free trade area over a transitional period lasting, in principle, a maximum of 10 years and in accordance with the WTO rules.

Between the main principles of the FTA, as foreseen in the negotiating directives, are those of asymmetry and differentiation. These twin concepts reflect the developmental approach of the proposed agreement. In recognition of South Africa's economic restructuring efforts currently under way, the Community will open up its market faster and more extensively for South Africa products than it will ask South Africa to do for Community products.

These principles are reflected in the different trade sectors, including textiles and clothing, where the Community will open its market faster and more extensively to South Africa than this country will open to the Community exports. In this particular sector, the principles of asymmetry and differentiation also reflect the different levels of competitivity of the industries of the two parties.

The Community aims to achieve the largest market access for its products into South Africa, including total free access, over an appropriate period of time. In exchange, it has offered to South Africa free access in a shorter period of time.

The results of the negotiations have been endorsed recently by the Heads of State or Government at the Berlin European Council.

Question No 57 by Richard Howitt (H−0310/99)

Subject: Investment in ACP countries

Paragraph 24 of resolution A5‐0508/98(6) , adopted in January this year, calls on the Commission to ensure that its development strategy to strengthen the private sector environment in developing countries specifically integrates the role of European‐based MNEs, and to progress an investment agreement with the ACP to promote economic growth and poverty reduction. Does the near future development agenda of the Commission include any initiatives to monitor European business and industry investment in the ACP countries through promoting codes of conduct so as to guarantee a coherent and transparent development policy that supports local development as well as foreign investment?

Answer

1. The Commission has taken an increasing interest in matters concerning the role of multinational companies investing in developing countries. This is a logical result of the growing interest in stimulating the role of private investment in the context of development policies. The importance of the role of the private sector, including the support to foreign direct investment, has today become a major issue in the Commission's policy dialogue with developing countries. This applies in particular to the currently ongoing negotiations with the ACP countries on the successor agreement to the Lomé Convention.

2. With the emphasis on the importance of the private sector and foreign investments there is an equal growing attention to the behaviour and role of foreign companies in developing countries. In the post‐Lomé negotiations there is a prominent place for the definition of fair and social policies and the role of civil society in the implementation of development policies.

3. Thus the post‐Lomé debate seems to be laying the basis for a policy where the support to investment goes hand in hand with a growing attention for the conduct of private sector investors. The Commission will consider monitoring mechanisms as requested in the Parliament's resolution A5‐0508/99 in the frame of the above‐mentioned ongoing negotiations and the future cooperation with ACP countries.

4. Finally, it should be mentioned that the Commission is actively participating in the review of the OECD guidelines for multinational enterprises and supports the view that the principles laid down in those guidelines should be observed world‐wide by our Member States. It is also taking an active interest in company‐based codes of conduct and helps notably NGOs to discuss, sensitise and assess the conduct of multinational investors and it supports awareness campaigns in developing countries (through budget line B7‐6000‐NGO‐co‐financing). However, the Commission stresses the importance of the voluntary nature of ‘codes of conduct' and of ‘social labelling'. As mentioned in earlier replies to Parliament the Commission favours a policy of the ‘carrot' and not that of the ‘stick'. It prefers multilateral agreements to unilateral rules. The Commission favours the implementation of existing international rules such as those developed by the ILO.

Question No 58 by Patricia McKenna (H−0223/99)

Subject: Foreign lecturers employed by Italian Universities

Can the Commission confirm that it received strong protests from the lecturers involved in this situation, alleging that inaccurate and incomplete information was being sent by the Italian authorities to the Commission concerning proceedings under Article 169?

Can it further confirm that one of the lecturers involved formally served the Commission with a copy of a complaint addressed to the Italian Director of Public Prosecutions concerning the transmission of this questioned information to the Commission?

What steps does the Commission intend to take concerning the lecturers' fears that inaccurate and incomplete information is being processed with regard to the Article 169 proceedings?

Will the Commission, in the interests of transparency and natural justice, use its discretionary powers to give the lecturers full access to all documents concerning the Article 169 proceedings?

Will the Commission confirm and explain that it changed its pleadings in law without notifying the lecturers who are the complainants?

Answer

The Commission is fully aware of the fact that the lecturers consider that it has been misled by the Italian authorities. Furthermore, a copy of a complaint from the lecturers to the Italian Prosecutor alleging the transmission of false and misleading information by the Italian authorities was handed over to the Commission at the end of January.

However, the Commission wishes to emphasise that, during the course of the proceedings, all information supplied by the Italian authorities has been carefully analysed and contrasted with the information supplied by the lecturers. On the basis of this permanent assessment of facts and evidence, the Commission considers that the information as a whole, which it has processed provides a reasonably accurate and complete picture. It must also be pointed out that, even in advance of receiving correspondence from the lecturers, complaining about the information supplied by the Italian authorities, the Commission had decided to send a supplementary reasoned opinion to Italy. This decision was taken after a full analysis of that information and precisely because the Commission did not accept the contention of the Italian authorities regarding compliance with Community law in the relevant universities.

Infringement proceedings under Article 169 of the Treaty are brought against a Member State and complainants may not be considered as parties to the infringement proceedings.

Furthermore, the Commission considers that giving individuals access to the infringement file would be contrary to the public interest within the meaning of the exceptions contained in the annex to the Commission Decision 94/90/ECSC/EC/EURATOM of 8 February 1994 on public access to Commission documents. Case law of the Court of First Instance has stated that the pre‐litigious correspondence between the Commission and a Member State, relating to investigations which may lead to an infringement procedure, falls under the heading of the protection of the public interest and thus warrants a refusal of access. The Court of First Instance has also confirmed that the public interest exception under the Code of Conduct is a mandatory exception and, thus, the Commission is obliged to refuse access to documents falling under such an exception. Given this clear jurisprudence, it has been the Commission's position that, if pre‐litigious correspondence between a Member State and the Commission is confidential and falls within the public interest exception, then a fortiori, the actual litigious pleadings based on Article 169 of the Treaty are similarly protected.

It should also be mentioned, however, that this issue is currently the subject of legal proceedings before the Court of First Instance, in case T−309/97, where a complainant is challenging the Commission's refusal to deliver a copy of a draft reasoned opinion.

As regards the Commission's legal position in the present case, it must be noted that the main claim which the Commission is putting forward in all its legal documents is the alleged infringement of Article 48 of the Treaty caused by the administrative practice of certain universities which seem not to have respected the acquired rights of the former language lecturers. The Commission's basic legal position has therefore remained unchanged since the beginning of the infringement proceedings.

Question No 59 by Hugh McMahon (H−0306/99)

Subject: Foreign language lettori working in Italian universities

In its opinion (Rule 147) for the Petitions Committee on the situation of foreign language lettori of 3 July 1997 (PE 222.503/fin), Parliament's Employment and Social Affairs Committee requested that specific enquiries be made by the Commission to the Italian government concerning complaints coming from lettori at the universities of Verona, Naples, Salerno and Bologna where there have been mass sackings.

Can the Commission report on these four specific universities and can the Commission confirm that these four universities will be used in its pleadings in law under the Article 169 proceedings currently in progress?

Answer

Further to the opinion of the Social Affairs Committee on foreign language lecturers in Italy, the Commission gathered a large amount of information concerning the situation at the universities of Verona, Naples, Salerno and Bologna.

Such information has been used in the context of the infringement proceedings against Italy.

The information and documentation collected by the Commission showed that the situation was not always clear in all universities. In many cases, the dispute between the lecturers and their university involved issues relating to national law together with issues relating to Community law.

On the basis of earlier cases decided by the Court of Justice concerning the position of the foreign language lecturers in Italian universities the Commission has come to the view that Italy has the right to organise and reorganise its public service, including professors and lecturers at universities, as it deems necessary. Such organisation or reorganisation falls foul of Community law, however, if it perpetuates the discrimination against EU citizens and does not respect their acquired rights. Therefore, the Commission in its complementary letter of formal notice and complementary reasoned opinion has concentrated on the situation in nine selected universities, including the University of Naples, where the lack of respect for the acquired rights was, in the Commission's view, the most obvious.

The Commission believes that it presently has sufficient proof in hand to bring the infringement procedure to a successful conclusion. Introducing the other universities mentioned in the question into the procedure would lead to further delays and complications which would not be in the interest of the Community and the individuals concerned.

The Commission will pursue the infringement proceedings until a solution which is in full conformity with Community law is found.

Question No 60 by Maj Theorin (H−0229/99)

Subject: Western Sahara

Like other colonised nations, the people of Western Sahara are entitled to self determination and independence, but Morocco, which for nearly 24 years has occupied Western Sahara in breach of the UN Charter and international law, stands in the way. It has hampered, delayed and blocked the UN peace plan. Nevertheless, the EU is giving Morocco substantial economic aid.

What action does the Commission intend to take in support of the Western Saharan people's right to self‐determination and independence, and how does it reconcile EU aid to Morocco with the latter's occupation of Western Sahara?

Answer

The Commission is closely monitoring the implementation of the plan to resolve the Western Sahara conflict that was adopted by the United Nations Security Council. Moreover, as the Council presidency confirmed in a statement on 29 December 1998, we fully support all efforts made by the Secretary‐General of the United Nations to find a fair, equitable and lasting solution for Western Sahara.

As far as the humanitarian aspects of this conflict are concerned, the Commission is continuing to supply substantial food and medical aid to the refugees in the Tindouf region through its NGO partners. The most recent instalment of this aid was adopted by the Commission on 20 October 1998 and totals EUR 10 million.

However, the financial assistance that the Community provides to Morocco comes under the Euro‐Mediterranean partnership that was launched in Barcelona in November 1995. This Euro‐Mediterranean initiative is not intended to replace the plan to resolve the conflict in Western Sahara that was adopted and that is currently being implemented by the United Nations Security Council.

Question No 61 by Ulla Sandbæk (H−0230/99)

Subject: Development aid and Morocco

Morocco is the greatest, or one of the greatest, recipients of EU development aid (ten years ago it was Ethiopia/Eritrea).

Exactly how much does Morocco receive every year (from all sources in the Budget)?

How does this priority for Morocco relate to poverty‐oriented policies of the Maastricht Treaty and the EU?

Answer

The total amount allocated for the financial and technical accompanying measures that Morocco has benefited from since the MEDA regulation(7) was adopted comes to EUR 454.48 million for 1996, 1997 and 1998. The funds are used, in particular, to support economic transition and sustainable economic and social development.

These measures are more than just development aid and come under the terms of the Euro‐Mediterranean partnership that was established by the 15 Member States of the European Union and the 12 Mediterranean countries. One of the main aims of this partnership is to set up a Euro‐Mediterranean free trade area.

The allocation of resources from the Community budget to the various third countries involved is determined by the budgetary authority.

Subject: Aid for the Kingdom of Morocco

Since 1996 the Kingdom of Morocco has received Euro 140 million a year from the EU. Each year its continued occupation of Western Sahara costs a huge amount, and it is continually delaying the referendum proclaimed by the UN on the freedom and independence of Western Sahara. Is the Commission therefore planning to cut back or even completely stop the provision of funds to Morocco?
