As far as the humanitarian aspects of this conflict are concerned, the Commission is continuing to supply substantial food and medical aid to the refugees in the Tindouf region through its NGO partners. The most recent instalment of this aid was adopted by the Commission on 20 October 1998 and totals EUR 10 million.

However, the financial assistance that the Community provides to Morocco comes under the Euro‐Mediterranean partnership that was launched in Barcelona in November 1995. This Euro‐Mediterranean initiative is not intended to replace the plan to resolve the conflict in Western Sahara that was adopted and that is currently being implemented by the United Nations Security Council.

Question No 63 by Jonas Sjöstedt (H−0239/99)

Subject: EU security service

According to reports in the media, some of the staff of the EU's security service are members of extreme right‐wing organisations or have contact with them. Can the Commission confirm that this is the case? If so, does the Commission have any plans for measures to rectify the situation?

Answer

The Commission does not keep any records of whether or not members of its staff – including the staff of the security service – belong to political organisations or parties.

The Staff Regulations state that a member of staff"s personal file must not include any details concerning his or her political, philosophical or religious views.

Question No 64 by Renzo Imbeni (H−0240/99)

Subject: Discrimination suffered by a disabled person on a domestic airline

Recently a domestic airline in a Member State refused to accept a reservation by a disabled person bearing the usual WCHC ticketing code and thus prevented the person from travelling. The reason given was that a person in a wheelchair can be transported only when accompanied by an able‐bodied passenger capable of assisting him, even though the person concerned had always travelled unaccompanied on other airlines. Does the Commission believe that this is consistent with Community legislation and the need to prevent discrimination against the disabled?

Answer

In many airports WCHC (wheel chair carry‐on, carry‐off) passengers need manual help, for example, to board the aircraft and ensure access for their wheelchair. Council Directive 90/269/EEC of 29 May 1990(8) on manual handling of loads forbids airline personnel (among others) to carry heavy loads such as an electric wheelchair or its owner. Some European carriers therefore require that another person who is able to assist must accompany WCHC passengers.

Existing Community legislation therefore does not prevent the airlines from requiring that WCHC passengers be accompanied by another person. It is, nevertheless, the opinion of the Commission that the passenger should be properly informed about this at the time of booking of the ticket, and not when he or she is actually presenting himself for check‐in.

The Commission is presently working on a Communication on passengers' rights in the air transport sector. Several relevant issues, including those relating to the provision of proper information to passengers whose mobility is impaired because of physical disability will certainly be covered in that publication. After public consultation, the Commission will give consideration to the need for legislative initiatives on these and other issues.

Question No 65 by Linda McAvan (H−0244/99)

Subject: Anti‐racism training for customs and immigration officials

Members of the European Parliament have on many occasions called attention to the racist and xenophobic behaviour which EU citizens of ethnic minority origin often encounter when exercising their right to move freely across internal EU borders.

Can the Commission indicate whether it supports or would support an initiative to give customs and immigration officials in both the EU and the applicant countries anti‐racism training within existing European funding programmes such as Phare and the Odysseus programme?

Answer

The political conditions for accession to the Union, agreed by the Copenhagen European Council, stipulate that candidate countries have to achieve stability of institutions guaranteeing human rights and respect for the protection of minorities.

The institution building and training projects in the field of justice and home affairs under the PHARE programme include elements which seek to address the need for protecting human rights, including an equal treatment of minorities. These issues are covered in particular in the projects on transposition of the asylum acquis and police training on immigration matters which were launched earlier this year.

As regards measures within the Union, the Odysseus programme, which covers cooperation in the areas of asylum, immigration and arrangements governing the crossing of external borders, can be used to fund measures aimed at staff responsible for carrying out border controls, not least with a view to ensuring better compliance with the principles governing human rights and the protection of minorities. Where appropriate, the measures funded may involve the applicant countries.

Question No 66 by MaLou Lindholm (H−0249/99)

Subject: Closure of smoke‐houses

According to information from the Food Administration and other sources, several small fish smoke‐ houses have been forced to close and even more are at risk. Small‐scale fisheries and small and medium‐sized smoke‐houses have a tremendously long history in Sweden and for decades have contributed to employment and small‐scale production in small coastal and island communities. As the EC directives allow exemptions for example for small‐scale poultry operations (92/116/EEC)(9) and meat products (92/5/EEC)(10) the Food Administration's interpretation was that the small‐scale exemption also applied to fish. An EU inspection of such premises revealed, without reasons, that this was not so. People in this trade are now being forced to either make expensive alterations to their premises (which most of them cannot afford) or to go out of business and become unemployed. At present DG VI is reviewing Directive 91/493/EEC(11) on health conditions.

Will the Commission's amended proposal allow general exemptions for small establishments, and can the small smoke‐houses and fish wholesalers affected be granted a stay of their alterations and exemptions until the new directive has been adopted?

Answer

The health conditions for the production and the placing on the market of fishery products are governed by Directive 91/493/EEC. This Directive fixes a high level of health protection and allows for derogations in the following cases:

1. A derogation from the structural requirements for establishments. The possibility for granting such derogation has however expired on 31 December 1995.

2. Small establishments which distribute on the local market and are situated in regions subject to particular supply constraints. For that purpose, the Commission has submitted, on 16 December 1992, a proposal to the Council in order to fix the minimal conditions to be respected by small establishments in certain parts of the Community. This has resulted in derogations for small establishments in Greece. However, the possibilities for exploiting this procedure have expire don 1 July 1992.

As the honourable Member of Parliament is pointing out, the Commission is preparing a proposal for a single hygiene text applying to all food, including fishery products. In this context the need for possible derogations will also be examined. The proposal will be submitted later this year, but its adoption may take a considerable amount of time.

The Commission would like to point out, however, that Directive 91/493/EEC does not apply to retail sales and direct transfers on local markets of small quantities by fishermen to retailers or consumers. Theses sales and transfers must be subject to health checks laid down by national rules. It may be worthwhile to explore whether the production of smoked fish referred to by the honourable Member of Parliament falls within this category.

Question No 67 by Hubert Pirker (H−0253/99)

Subject: Migratory movements in the EU

On 22 July 1998 the Commission submitted proposals amending Regulation 1612/68/EEC(12) and Directive 68/360/EEC(13) (COM(98)0394/final)(14) . The new versions regard the reunification of families in an entirely new light, both qualitatively and quantitatively. A number of questions remained unanswered in the discussion of my opinion for the Committee on Civil Liberties and Internal Affairs.

What actual effect have the provisions on freedom of movement had on movements of people within the EU?

What investigations have there been of the actual extent of migratory movements in the European Union?

In the Commission's opinion, how large is the group of persons referred to in Article 10(1)(c) of the Commission proposal amending Regulation 1612/68/EEC?

In view of the provisions in the new Article 11 of Regulation 1612/68/EEC whereby such persons are given access to the labour market, consideration must be given to the effects of this right so that there is no additional strain on social security systems.

Answer

The provisions on free movement of workers have been in force for the past 30 years. Nonetheless, only around 5 million Europeans have exercised their right to free movement and live permanently in another Member State (including all categories of persons: employed and self‐employed workers, retired and inactive people and students).

Cultural, social and linguistic barriers remain and the legislation has not resulted in major migration flows within the Union during the past thirty years.

As regards the Commission's proposals for amending Regulation 1612/68 on free movement of workers, Article 10.1(c) of the Commission's proposal only covers family members who already form part of the family household of a Community worker within the Union. First entry in one Member State of such family members, when they are third country nationals, would still depend on national immigration law.

The objective of this amendment is to ensure that a family household which is already formed within a Member State is not broken up due to the fact that the worker decides to exercise his/her right to free movement. Member States have no data on the number of people affected, but the Commission has no indication that the total number is significant.

As regards the impact of the proposed Article 11 of Regulation 1612/68 on the national labour markets and national social security systems, family members of Community workers who are European nationals already have a direct right to work. The new provision would essentially benefit third country family members who were not previously covered by the family reunification rules of Regulation 1612/68.

Given the limited number of persons who would be covered by this new provision, it cannot be expected that there would be a significant impact – either positive or negative – on social security systems.

Question No 68 by Philippe Monfils (H−0255/99)

Subject: Mismanagement of the Clovis crèche

The Clovis crèche, which has achieved notoriety as a result of the serious offences alleged to have been committed there by paedophiles, is managed by a company that has no expertise in this domain and does not comply with the contract specifications, with the result that there is high staff turn‐over, the prescribed ratio of staff to children is not observed, absurdly punitive methods of controlling the children are employed, and the meals provided consist of frozen, rather than fresh, food. Despite these irregularities, the contract has been extended until June 1999.

Does the Commission agree that it is essential either to cancel the contract on the grounds of the company's failure to meet its contractual obligations, or at least not to extend it beyond June 1999?

Answer

The company that manages the Clovis crèche was selected following an invitation to tender that established the technical conditions for its management on the basis of contract specifications. The subcontracting agreement for the management of the crèche was signed on 23 June 1995.

As regards the points made by the honourable Member concerning non‐compliance with the contract specifications, I would make the following comments:

‐ in terms of the high staff turn‐over, 87 % of the nursery nurses were recruited between 1995 and 1997, and 49 % of them have worked there since 1995, when the contract with Esedra was signed,

‐ as far as the ratio of staff to children is concerned, a nursery nurse with at least three years" experience is present in every room, in accordance with the contract specifications;

‐ as regards the methods used to punish the children, the Commission has received no specific, detailed complaints about these. In view of the serious nature of such accusations and so that action can be taken at management level, including legal action if necessary, the administration must have conclusive information about the events and those involved. We have asked the parents" association for assistance here;

‐ as for the fact that the meals provided consist of frozen food, the contract specifications do allow this and it is also permitted by the national childcare organisation.

The regular checks carried out by the Commission have not uncovered any serious instances where the company has failed to comply with the contract specifications to an extent that would justify immediately terminating the contract. Nevertheless, the Commission is continuing to monitor the application of the contract specifications in order to determine what attitude it should take throughout the duration of the contract.

Question No 69 by Esko Seppänen (H−0257/99)

Subject: EU Staff Regulations

What is the Commission's view on the question whether the same person may legally receive both a salary and a pension from an EU institution? If this should prove to be illegal, what does the Commission propose to do pursuant to Article 232 of the Treaty in the event of such a case arising?

Answer

Article 40 of Annex VIII of the Staff Regulations of the European Communities states that ‘a retirement or invalidity pension shall not be paid concurrently with the salary payable by an institution of the three European Communities nor with the allowance payable under Articles 41 and 50 of the Staff Regulations". This article does not allow for any legal interpretation that would enable an official to draw both a salary and a Community retirement or invalidity pension. However, if such a case were to arise, the Commission would rectify the situation in accordance with Article 41 of Annex VIII of the Staff Regulations and would recover the undue payment pursuant to Article 85 of the Staff Regulations. Possible disciplinary proceedings might also be considered.

The Commission carries out regular checks designed to detect cases such as this, while at interinstitutional level, software that was introduced in 1998 should prevent any such errors a priori through preliminary checks on a proportion of those beginning work in the Community institutions.

Moreover, the Commission does not see any link between Article 232 of the EC Treaty and Community salaries and pensions.

Question No 70 by Elly Plooij‐van Gorsel (H−0258/99)

Subject: Compatibility of the French Electricity Act with the European electricity directive

On 2 March 1998 I tabled questions to the Commission on (proposed) French legislation concerning liberalisation of the electricity market which referred to the transfer of the electricity transmission network by the French state to EDF at an inappropriate transfer price. This would increase, in an unlawful way, EDF's capital.

The Commission replied that it was not aware of this legislation, but that it would look into the matter I had raised. Can the Commission inform me of the results of this inquiry?

The French Electricity Act which has now come into force contains a provision to the effect that customers can change electricity suppliers only once every three years. Does this clause infringe the competition rules in general and the electricity directive in particular?

Answer

The Commission has not as yet concluded its examination of the measures taken by the French authorities in transferring the general transmission network to EDF. It is looking at this issue in the context of its analysis of the various measures taken by the Member States to liberalise the electricity market in line with Directive 96/92. The Commission will take the necessary steps if it becomes evident that the measures taken by the French authorities contravene Community law.

The directive does not contain any explicit provisions on the duration of contracts relating to the provision of electricity. As far as the Commission is concerned, this is a matter of freedom of choice which may come under the competition rules set out in the EC Treaty. The Commission is aware of an amendment adopted during the vote at first reading in the National Assembly requiring companies to agree to stay with the same electricity supplier for a minimum of three years. It is possible that by imposing a minimum period of three years, the authorities are introducing an unjustifiable restriction to the liberalisation of the market that is required by Directive 96/92, and are contravening Articles 5, 85 and 90 of the Treaty. The Commission will express an official view on this matter if the amendment in question is included in the final version of the law.

Question No 71 by Eva Kjer Hansen (H−0262/99)

Subject: Infringement proceedings against Denmark over the registration tax

In its reasoned opinion of 18 May 1998 the Commission took the view that the provisions of Sections 114 and 115 in the Danish Ministry of Transport's registration order conflicted with Article 48 of the EC Treaty concerning the free movement of workers and Article 59 on the freedom to provide services, as they prevented persons residing in Denmark from using foreign‐registered company cars for business and private use in Denmark.

How does the Commission intend to pursue the matter to ensure that the provisions of the Treaty are implemented?

Would the Commission consider it sufficient a satisfactory solution for authorisation to be issued for the partial use of company cars in return for a periodic or daily charge, would it regard such double taxation of the vehicle as an unreasonably discriminatory arrangement?

Answer

Current Danish rules forbid the use of company cars registered abroad by crossborder workers who reside in Denmark. The Commission is of the opinion that these rules are incompatible with Articles 48 and 59 of the EC Treaty, and infringement proceedings against Denmark have been launched, following a complaint.

As part of these proceedings, the Danish proposal for amendments to laws on registration of motor vehicles is being examined by the Commission, and the question of subjecting companies to double registration will be examined in this context.

The Commission will keep the honourable Member informed.

Question No 72 by Nuala Ahern (H−0263/99)

Subject: Fourth report on the present situation and prospects for radioactive waste management in the European Union

In the Commission Communication and Fourth Report on the present situation and prospects for radioactive waste management in the European Union (COM(98)0799/final, of 11 January 1999), the Commission refers to numerous past Council resolutions dating back to 1989 on which the strategy has been promulgated, for instance in section 1.8. Why has the Commission made no reference at all to Parliament's report on the transport and storage of radioactive waste by rapporteur Mr Llewellyn Smith, A3‐0220/93(15) , adopted with amendments by Parliament on 16 July 1993? Answer

Question No 73 by Alexander Falconer (H−0264/99/rev. 1)

Subject: Ethnic cleansing in the former Yugoslavia

According to World Bank figures, prior to ‘ethnic cleansing', between 600 000 and 1.9 million workers out of a total workforce of 2.7 million in the former Yugoslavia were declared redundant under the US loan‐pressured Financial Operations Act, and GDP subsequently declined by 50 % as a result of the privatisation programme. Can the Commission confirm these figures, and, if so, can it indicate whether, in its opinion, this action was a factor in the ethnic cleansing operations?

Answer

The Commission cannot answer the honourable Member's question. Apart from the absence of dates or clear references, the question is not clear if it concerns the FRY or rather the countries of ‘former Yugoslavia'. In either case, the state has never ‘declared' workers ‘redundant'.

The near 50 % decline in GDP in the period between 1991 and the present, is due to war, sanctions, economic mismanagement and the dismantlement of the former SFRY economy, and certainly not to privatisation (which has not really begun) or to any single economic decision.

In any case, whatever the impact of the events mentioned, or indeed of any economic event in the SFRY or the FRY in the last decade, the Commission is of the firm opinion that such events are in no way responsible for the nature of the war which followed.

The responsibility for the ethnic cleansing and the vicious nature of the war in the former Yugoslavia lies with the authorities in power.

Question No 74 by John Cushnahan (H−0265/99)

Subject: Roaccutane

Is the Commission aware that concerns have been raised regarding possible side effects of the drug Roaccutane? What action does the Commission propose to take in relation to these concerns?

Answer

The Commission is aware that concerns have been raised regarding possible side effects of the medicinal product called Roaccutane, the active ingredient of which is Isotretinoin.

The medicinal product has been marketed in most of the countries in the Community since 1983. The use of the medicinal product is restricted to the management of severe acne which has failed to respond to other therapies. The medicine is available on prescription only, and in some countries its use is restricted to use by specialist dermatologists.

Since the time of first marketing, Roaccutane has been strictly contraindicated in women where there is any risk of pregnancy, since it is known to be teratogenic.

More recently new information on psychological side effects has emerged, and these side effects have been extensively discussed within the Pharmacovigilance working party of the Committee for Proprietary Medicinal Products during the period from March 1998 until October 1998.

Following these discussions, variations to the terms of the marketing authorisation have been introduced in all Member States where the product is marketed, including special warnings on the risks of depression, suicide or suicidal tendencies and the need for particular care to be taken with respect to the monitoring of patients who have been prescribed this medicine. These warnings have been introduced into the prescribing information and the patient information leaflets. In addition, as is normal with all marketed medicinal products, the national authorities continue to actively monitor safety data on Roaccutane.

The Commission is therefore satisfied that the potentially very serious side effects of this medicinal product are well known and are being continually monitored by all Member States where the medicinal product is marketed and that measures are in place to assure additional follow‐up on new information if necessary.

Question No 75 by Mihail Papayannakis (H−0269/99)

Subject: Protection of the designation feta

In a recent judgment, the Court of Justice annulled the provision of the Regulation establishing feta as a protected designation of origin. Without wishing to interfere in the Court's proceedings, I believe it is imperative to ask the Commission for its opinion as to the accuracy of certain arguments put to the Court and to the public via the press. It was claimed, for example, that feta is a cheese produced from cow's, sheep's and goat's milk. What ‘scientific' study produced this finding when it is a well‐known fact that neither fresh nor powdered cow's milk has absolutely anything to do with feta? Does the Commission intend to reformulate the wording of the Regulation to make absolutely clear this distinction, which is important in terms of technology and flavour and will also help consumers recognise whether they are buying just any ‘white' cheese or feta and will protect genuine feta producers?

Answer

In accordance with the Greek regulation of 1994 governing practices that are 2000 years old, the name ‘feta" is reserved as a protected designation of origin for cheese produced in a specific geographic region and made exclusively with sheep"s milk or a mixture of sheep"s milk and goat"s milk according to a traditional method.

The Commission confirmed this protection for the name ‘feta" at Community level through Regulation (EC) No 1107/96.

On 16 March 1999, the Court of Justice partially annulled this regulation on the grounds that the Commission had not given sufficient consideration to the situation in the other Member States. It should be noted here that although production of this cheese in France respects the traditional quality requirements mentioned above, the feta cheese produced in Denmark and Germany is mainly made with cow"s milk. However, since Directive 94/36/EC was adopted, the use of colouring agents to whiten feta cheese, which is initially slightly yellow, has been banned.

Following the Court"s judgement and in view of the guidelines it has set out, the Commission must now review the entire ‘feta" problem and comply with the restrictions on substance and procedures that are laid down in Regulation (EC) No 2081/92. It would therefore be premature to give a definitive response to the honourable Member"s question.

Question No 76 by Jörn Svensson (H−0270/99)

Subject: Impact of budget spending on employment

For two years in succession the EU has entered EURO 150 m in its budget for employment. What results have these funds produced? How many jobs have been created, in which industries and in which Member States?

Answer

The Growth and Employment Initiative (which committed 120 m ECU in 1998 and 150 m ECU in 1999 and year 2000, respectively) covers a programme of financial assistance for innovative and job‐creating SMEs. It aims at stimulating job creation by facilitating and strengthening the establishment and growth of SMEs, by supporting their investment activity through increased availability of finance.

The Commission has implemented three types of facilities: the EFT start‐up Facility and the SME Guarantee Facility – on which the Commission has already initiated negotiations with Member States to set up and select financial intermediaries – and the JEV Facility . These facilities are currently in the implementation phase and, therefore, it would be premature to give a definite answer at this stage. The issue concerning the number of jobs created by these facilities will be addressed during the monitoring and evaluation phases. Their results will be included, as far as possible, in the annual reports that the Commission will present to the Parliament and the Council.

The European Employment Strategy establishes the orientation for priorities and focus of future employment policy at national and Community level. The targets and objectives of the Employment Guidelines set the course for their action while Community financial support is provided by the Structural Funds. The European Social Fund, in particular, has a specific mandate to financially support the European Employment Strategy. The main responsibility, however, for implementing the European Employment Strategy remains with the Member States.

Question No 77 by Gerard Collins (H−0271/99)

Subject: Equality of opportunity in employment

The most recently published employment statistics from Eurostat show that while the rate of unemployment among men across the Union has decreased from 8.8 % to 8.5 % since 1992, the unemployment rate of women has actually increased from 11.4 % to 11.7 %. Can the Commission state what emphasis it has been putting on the need to promote equality of opportunity in its recent assessment of the national action plans?

Answer

In 1998, for the first time, Member States drafted National Action Plans (NAPs) in which they presented the way in which they were implementing the 1998 Employment Guidelines. In a communication published in May 1998(16) , the Commission examined the commitments made by the Member States as regards the four pillars of the Guidelines, including equal opportunities. Generally, the responses of Member States demonstrated that there was a clear commitment to making progress on employment. A number of positive elements could be discerned. All Member States had taken the issue of equal opportunities on board. Preparing the NAPs had raised awareness of the importance of equal opportunities policy. However the principal focus in the Plans was on the first two pillars, 'employability' and ‘entrepreneurship'. On the fourth pillar, the content of the NAPs addressing the issue of equality between women and men appeared to be modest and insufficiently detailed. This was reflected in the relatively limited number of new measures proposed, the small budgets attributed (if mentioned), and the limited number of quantitative targets envisaged.

The Joint Employment Report(17) of 14 October 1998 presented an analysis of the implementation of the NAPs and a series of good practices in Member States. The Heads of State and Government at the European Council in Vienna in December 1998 considered this detailed examination. This led to the conclusion of the Vienna European Council that ‘employment is the top priority of the European Union' and that equal opportunities for women and men are a vital component of the whole employment strategy.

Member States have agreed to submit by 31 May 1999 a report, describing both the implementation of the 1998 National Action Plans and the adjustments to be made to it in 1999, inter alia, to take account of the changes introduced by the 1999 Guidelines.

The 1999 Employment Guidelines(18) concerning equal opportunities between men and women have been strengthened as compared with the 1998 Guidelines: Member States are urged to mainstream equality between women and men in all employment policy and labour market measures. This should allow the adoption of a gender‐mainstreaming approach in implementing the Guidelines. Furthermore, Member States will, as part of their policy to tackle gender gaps, also strive to promote equal pay for equal work or work of equal value and to diminish gender differentials in incomes. They will consider an increased use of measures for the advancement of women. The 1999 Guidelines also attach more importance to the reconciliation of work and family: Member States will design, implement and promote family‐friendly policies, including affordable, accessible and high quality care services for children and other dependants, as well as parental and other leave schemes.

Question No 78 by Mark Killilea (H−0291/99)

Subject: Fourth action programme on equal opportunities for men and women

Can the Commission comment on the fact that it is about to close down the Technical Assistance Office which implements the fourth action Programme for equal opportunities for men and women in the EU and give its views on the future of those projects that are currently under way in the Member States under the action Programme?

Answer

The Commission decided not to renew, beyond 31 March 1999, the contract concluded with the technical assistance office which was helping the Commission to implement certain activities under the fourth medium‐term Community action programme for equal opportunities between women and men (1996‐2000); the contract in question had expired.

The Commission has now taken direct responsibility for monitoring the projects under way in the Member States and promoting the programme as a whole.

Question No 79 by Inger Schörling (H−0274/99)

Subject: EU grant for relocation of company within EU assisted regions

The American Lear Corporation has produced a plan which entails the closure of its plant at Bengtsfors in Dalsland, Sweden. The town will lose 860 jobs at a swoop. The company's car‐seat production will be transferred to a plant which it is planning to build in Portugal. By relocating to Portugal, the company will receive an EU grant of SEK 75 million and a tax concession from the Portuguese government to the same amount. In addition, the Lear Corporation previously received an EU grant to maintain production at Bengtsfors and is now to receive a grant to move it away. Is it reasonable for EU funds to be used to move production from one assisted region to another? Surely only new production set‐ups should qualify for grants under EU subsidy rules? If this is not the case, should such a rule not be introduced? Does the Commission accept tax competition of this kind despite the fact that tax competition is contrary to the code of conduct on corporate taxation adopted in 1997? Does the Commission intend to investigate more closely the circumstances under which the Lear Corporation obtained the grant to transfer its production to Portugal? Are there, or have there been plans to produce statistics on the frequency with which EU grants result in companies moving their production from place to place within the EU without creating any more jobs?

Question No 80 by Jan Andersson (H−0286/99)

Subject: Commission efforts to prevent distortion of competition in the internal market

Lear, the motor vehicle components company, has transferred operations from Bengtsfors in Sweden to Palema in Portugal as a direct result of receiving EU aid. As Lear is a major employer in Bengtsfors, the closure will have very serious consequences for the whole district. All over Sweden, people are quite rightly up in arms about EU resources being used to shed jobs. The EU's inability to do more to ensure that structural aid and regional development grants do not distort competition is quite unacceptable.

What action can the Commission take to prevent structural aid from being used as direct aid to companies and thus from contributing to a distortion of competition which results in jobs being lost at other locations which are in need of jobs, as has happened at Bengtsfors?

Question No 81 by Yvonne Sandberg‐Fries (H−0288/99)

Subject: Internal market and relocation aid

The Lear motor parts factory in the Swedish industrial town of Bengtsfors has decided to relocate parts of its operation to Palema outside Lisbon (Portugal). It appears that one reason for the decision was that it will give the firm access to higher levels of EU aid. Lear is a major employer in Bengtsfors, and the closure will hit the local community and its inhabitants very hard. Many people are currently indignant – and rightly so – at EU funds being spent to take away people's jobs. The public must be clearly informed of what measures are being taken to prevent structural fund/relocation aid being used as an inadmissible competitive advantage to entice firms away from another Member State.

Does the Commission agree that the use of structural aid in connection with Lear's relocation from Sweden to Portugal may be seen as having contributed to a distortion of competition, thereby conflicting with the very spirit of the EU's regional policy? What measures does the Commission propose to take to ensure that the use of structural fund and relocation aid does not result in the kind of distortion of competition we have seen in the case of Lear?

Joint answer

In a Community where one of the basic principles is the freedom of establishment, any enterprise can decide to set up where it wants to or to transfer its activity from one area to another. Most often, this kind of decision is taken in a broader context of rationalisation or development of an investment plan. The elements which are taken into account by a firm in deciding on localisation are numerous and, in general, unknown to the Commission. The possibility of obtaining aid is but one of them.

As regards national aid for regional development purposes, the Commission considers that this aid is compatible with the single market and in conformity with Articles 92‐94 of the EC Treaty if it conforms with the rules adopted by the Commission under its competition policy. These rules restrict inter alia the geographical scope and the admissible intensity of the aid. At the request of the Member States and subject to the Commission's approval, Community Structural Funds may part‐finance the approved national aid. In doing so, the Structural Funds compensate for the lack of resources available in disfavoured regions to assist their enterprises.

Concerning the investment by Lear corporation in Setubal, Portugal, the Commission can confirm that, according to information provided by the Portuguese authorities, public aid of 10.7 million euro will be paid in accordance with the PEDIP programme approved by the Commission in 1994. Of this amount 75 % is co‐financed by the Structural Funds. Of these 8 million euro, 2.5 comes from the European Regional Development Fund and 5.5 from the European Social Fund. In addition, the Portuguese authorities have agreed to provide up to 5 million euro in fiscal credits. Setubal being a region which qualifies as a least‐favoured region both under Article EC 92.3.a and under Objective 1 of the Structural Funds, this assistance complies with the Community competition rules on State aid.

It should also be emphasised that, according to the information available to the Commission, the investment is part of a wider strategy of the enterprise to expand its activities in Europe. The investment in Portugal amounting to 73.5 million euro, it is expected to create 4022 new jobs. At the same time, the company operates a reorganisation of its production facilities in Sweden. This includes the transfer to Thailand of most of the sewing activities of the Bengstfors plant, representing 280 jobs, whereas the production and assembly activities of the plant are transferred to Göteborg, which is a non‐assisted area. These elements indicate that there is no direct link between the company's reorganisation in Sweden and its greenfield investment in Portugal. It follows that, in the present case, the Commission is not of the opinion that Community funding has been used for the relocation of jobs into Portugal at the expense of jobs in Sweden.

On a more general level, the Commission agrees that efforts should be made to discourage ‘subsidy shopping' by investors and to avoid investments being (re)located purely as a result of the availability of aid. What is more, the Commission has already taken concrete steps in that direction:

* In 1997, the Commission has approved new Guidelines on national regional aid. The new Guidelines contain a special provision requiring Member States to make the granting of regional aid conditional on the maintenance of the investment concerned for a minimum period of five years. Member States have until 1 January 2000 to bring their existing regional aid schemes in line with this new provision.

* With respect to the next programming period, the Commission has proposed, in Article 29 of the draft Regulation laying down general provisions of the Structural Funds, that the Member States shall ensure that an operation retains the contribution from the Funds only if that operation does not, within five years of the date of the decision of the competent national authorities or the managing authority on the contribution of the Funds, undergo a substantial modification resulting notably from a cessation or change in location of a productive activity.

* Finally, in the recently issued guidelines for the next programming period, there is a statement of principle that the Structural Funds should not be used for the mere relocation of existing activities.

Question No 82 by Vassilis Ephremidis (H−0276/99)

Subject: Urgent need for intervention to save the historic monastery of Apostolos Andreas in occupied Karpassia

The historic monastery of Apostolos Andreas in occupied Karpassia is in a dire situation and in immediate danger of collapse. This monument to the religious, historical and cultural heritage is the sole surviving monastery in the occupied territories; all the others have either fallen into ruin or have been converted into mosques by the occupying regime which is engaged in a campaign of cultural cleansing and falsification of the history of the occupied territories, allowing – or encouraging – looting, the sale of priceless objects, vandalism and destruction of historic sites, museums, works of art and churches.

Bearing in mind that the Monastery of Apostolos Andreas is not only important for Cyprus and Greek civilisation but is also part of the European and international religious, cultural and historical heritage, will the Commission say what measures it will take to activate and implement the agreements and programmes for the preservation and conservation of the cultural heritage and send a team of scientists and engineers to preserve and restore the monastery in question? What measures will it also take to protect the other historic and religious monuments, buildings and objects threatened with vandalism and destruction, which would deprive world civilisation of its inalienable riches?

Answer

The fourth financial protocol between Cyprus and the EC provides for the possibility of financing projects between the two communities and it is possible that the action suggested by the honourable Member could be included in that context. However, since December 1997, when Mr Denktash decided to suspend all contact between the two communities following the Luxembourg European Council, we have not been able to consider any projects for possible funding.

Question No 83 by Manuel Medina Ortega (H−0280/99)

Subject: Fishing agreement with Argentina

What action has the Commission taken to ensure compliance with the present fishing agreement with the Argentine Republic, and what safeguards are there for maintaining the activity of the Community fleet in those waters?

Answer

1. What action has the Commission taken to ensure that Argentina complies with the fisheries agreement?

The fisheries agreement between the EU and Argentina, which entered into force on 24 May 1994 for a period of 5 years, provides for the establishment of joint enterprises and temporary joint ventures.

A total of 27 joint enterprises have been set up involving 29 vessels that have been transferred permanently to Argentina; 15 of these fish for ‘hubbsi" hake. The three temporary joint ventures that were formed, involving three vessels, are now coming to an end and the vessels are returning to the Community.

Recently, since the entry into force of the fisheries agreement, Argentina"s catch of ‘hubbsi" hake has increased so much that its survival is threatened. As a result, the Argentinian Government has proclaimed a ‘fisheries emergency" and is adopting a series of management and conservation measures.

During the seventh meeting of the joint committee – held in Buenos Aires on 4 and 5 May 1998 – the parties recognised that the scarcity of the resources was not due to the activities of vessels that had been transferred to Argentina under the fisheries agreement, but rather to the inadequacies of the Argentinian system of managing and controlling its resources.

However, the Community has always supported any conservation measures aimed at ensuring that sustainable and rational use is made of resources. The Commission has often stressed that it supported all conservation efforts that were not discriminatory and that were adopted in a transparent manner on the basis of objective scientific criteria.

Through Resolution 2/99 of 5 January 1999, the Argentinian Government adopted a conservation measure reducing each vessel"s maximum catch for 1999 by 50 % compared with 1997. It also established three fishing zones according to the type of vessel.

First of all, this resolution affects the fishing activities of the vessels operating under the agreement. It was adopted without consulting the Community, either before or immediately following its adoption; it therefore contravenes Article 9(1) of the agreement.

Secondly, as regards its substance, the Community has established that this measure represents an average reduction of more than 65 % in the individual quotas allocated to these vessels. In addition, the Community considers that the establishment of fishing zones has no scientific basis and is not provided for in the agreement. It also prevents those involved from fully exercising the fishing rights they were granted under the agreement.

As a result, this resolution, which was adopted unilaterally by Argentina, disrupts the overall balance of the agreement.

As the Commission explained to the Committee on Fisheries at its meeting on 15 and 16 March 1999, it has made its position clear to the Argentinian authorities on several occasions, including in a letter of 25 January 1999 from Mr Cavaco, the Director‐General of DG XIV, to Mr Auguste, the Argentinian Deputy Secretary of State for Fisheries. In addition, talks were held on 2 February between Mrs Bonino, the European Commissioner, and Mr Alonso, the Argentinian Secretary of State for Fisheries, and a letter was sent on 1 March from Mrs Bonino and Vice‐President Marín, the European Commissioner, to the Argentinian Minister for Foreign Affairs, Mr Di Tella. Moreover, in accordance with Article 10 of the agreement, the Community asked that an extraordinary meeting of the joint committee be convened.

The letter from Vice‐President Marín and Mrs Bonino protested against the new measures adopted by Argentina and asked that the appropriate changes be made to preserve the overall balance of the agreement.

During the meeting of the joint committee, which was held in Buenos Aires on 4 and 5 March 1999, the Commission reasserted its position and asked that the reductions should be applied on the basis of the quotas allocated to each vessel and that the zones should not be introduced.

The Argentinian delegation showed no flexibility in trying to rectify the situation and reestablish the overall balance of the agreement. It reminded the Community that EUR 6 million had yet to be paid in the context of scientific and technical cooperation.

2. What safeguards are there for maintaining the activity of the Community fleet in Argentinian waters?

As the honourable Member is aware, under the agreement substantial financial aid was paid to Community vessel owners who formed a joint enterprise in Argentina with a view to transferring vessels there permanently. As a result, we cannot talk about a Community fleet that operates in Argentina; we should instead look on it as a section of the Argentinian fleet operating under the fisheries agreement that expires on 24 May 1999.

The vessels transferred under the fisheries agreement are Argentinian vessels and should benefit from the same safeguards as the rest of the Argentinian fleet. The Community will continue to ensure that these vessels are not discriminated against in any way, in accordance with the provisions of Article 9 of the fisheries agreement.

Question No 84 by John McCartin (H−0282/99)

Subject: Amnesty International claims concerning human rights in Egypt

Is the Commission aware of Amnesty International claims regarding a worsening human rights situation in Egypt, particularly in respect of the numbers of deaths and the torture of prisoners in custody? Has the Commission raised in its contacts with the Egyptian authorities the situation surrounding the death in 1996 of Karimi Nduthu, the Secretary‐General of the Release Political Prisoners Group, into which no inquest has yet been held?

Answer

The Commission is informed about the human rights situation in Egypt and is consistently monitoring developments. This includes the reports by Amnesty International and other human rights organisations concerning the question of alleged deaths and torture of prisoners in custody.

The Commission has not discussed the case of the death of Mr Karimi Nduthu with the Egyptian authorities. Karimi Nduthu, Secretary‐General of the Release Political Prisoners Group, was a Kenyan citizen; the Release Political Prisoners Group is a Kenyan human rights organisation and his death took place in Kenya. To the knowledge of the Commission there is no connection with Egypt.

Question No 85 by Karin Riis‐Jørgensen (H−0283/99)

Subject: Travel discounts granted on social grounds to certain sections of the population in Denmark

In Denmark, the State provides subsidies so that certain sections of the population (pensioners, those on national service, etc.) can travel at reduced rates or free on public transport.

The Danish government is required to send information to the Commission on its financial links with public‐sector undertakings, including financial remuneration for tasks which undertakings carry out for public authorities.

Is discrimination between passengers using public‐sector and private‐sector transport undertakings admissible with regard to travel discounts granted on social grounds? Will the Commission also state whether the Danish government has sent information on the subsidies paid to DSB (Danish State Railways) and other public‐sector transport undertakings in connection with such discounts?

Answer

As the honourable Member will know, different fares by different operators for particular social groups and for travelling at different times are normal in public transport. They are not, in themselves, evidence of discrimination by public authorities and they may result from the varying cost structures or pricing strategies of different operators.

Even where fare discounts are funded by state aids (in accordance with Regulation 1191/69), differences in the availability of aids for different types of operators may reflect sound transport policy judgments by the authorities that grant the aids, or they may be due to various acceptable priorities pursued by different competent authorities.

In general – and for obviously practical reasons – questions of possible discrimination are assessed at the national level. Community interest in such questions arises only when Community considerations are relevant particularly, for instance, where there is significant cross‐border operation of transport services.

The Danish authorities have only transmitted a figure for aids to Danish State Railways and the sums relate to a combination of aids for tariff obligations and for obligations to operate. For 1997 (the latest year for which figures are available) that total was 1, 671.1 million Danish krone.

The Commission does not ask for information about aids for other forms of passenger transport, such as buses and light rail, as these sectors are not liberalised in the Single Market and financial support is therefore not likely to distort competition in the Community.

Question No 86 by Lone Dybkjær (H−0287/99)

Subject: Blue Flag campaign

In 1998, the Commission decided to end its financial support for the Blue Flag campaign. The main reason for doing so, it said, was that it had received a large number of official complaints about the campaign. The Blue Flag coordinators repeatedly asked the Commission to let them know how many complaints had been received and what they were about, but the Commission refused to provide such information, citing the need to protect the plaintiffs. The Commission's manner of dealing with this matter raises a number of general questions concerning openness and fairness in connection with the allocation of support to NGOs:

Are organisations against which complaints have been lodged able to ascertain how many complaints have been received and what they are about?

Does the Commission have legal reasons for withholding such information?

Has the Commission considered that it might be possible to tell those against whom complaints have been lodged what the complaints are about without revealing the identity of the plaintiffs?

Answer

As the honourable Member refers to the issue of access to information relating to complaints about the Blue Flag campaign, the Commission replies to the questions concerning access to information in the light of this specific case.

The Commission received in 1998 some complaints about a possibly doubtful attribution of Blue Flags for some beaches. The letters raised issues related to criteria of the Blue Flag scheme, which falls outside the Commission's competence, where only water quality under the Bathing Water Directive is covered. Because the Commission has only the competence to control matters under Community law, it advised the complainants to refer their concerns to FEEE European coordinator, who is responsible for the Blue Flag campaign.

FEEE asked for information in the said letters. Pursuant to the rules in Commission Decision 94/90/EEC on public access to Commission documents(19) , an applicant for documents – in this case FEEE – is referred to the author if these documents originate from outside the Commission. However, as in the subject case authors are complainants, whose identity under the Commission rules on handling of complaints is confidential, FEEE could not be referred directly to the authors, but the Commission advised the authors to contact FEEE as explained above.

Question No 87 by Alan Gillis (H−0293/99)

Subject: Working time Directive

Given the continuing non‐respect of the working time Directive by the Health Boards in Ireland with regard to the working hours of non‐consultant hospital doctors and general medical practitioners employed by the Health Boards, in some cases as many as 108 hours per week, what measures will the Commission take to ensure an urgent end to this situation which is causing grave damage to the health of our medical practitioners working in these deplorable conditions and in the long term putting patient care at risk?

Answer

With regard to doctors, it should be noted that the provisions of the Working Time Directive (93/104/EC)(20) do not apply to ‘doctors in training'.

It is not clear whether the doctors referred to in the question fall under the definition of ‘doctors in training'. Since Member States are responsible for the implementation of Directives, complaints alleging breaches of national laws implementing a Directive should be addressed to the relevant national courts in the first instance.

Where it appears to the Commission that a Member State has failed to implement the provisions of a Directive, the Commission will investigate the matter.

The Commission has clearly stated that the working time of doctors in training needs to be reduced. That is why it has already proposed to apply the provisions of the Working Time Directive to them. Under the proposal(21) , weekly working time would be limited to a maximum of 54 hours on average for a transitional period of 7 years and to 48 hours on average thereafter. This proposal is presently being examined by the Council and the Parliament.

Question No 88 by James Fitzsimons (H−0296/99)

Subject: Coastal protection

Will the Commission indicate what action it proposes to take to promote greater emphasis on coastal protection policies, particularly in areas such as the east and south‐east coasts of Ireland?

Answer

The Commission's Demonstration Programme on Integrated Coastal Zone Management (ICZM) was launched in 1996 as a response to the Council Resolution of 6 May 1994 calling for a Community strategy on integrated coastal‐zone management (94/C 135/02).

The programme is based around 35 local and regional projects, a series of cross‐cutting thematic analyses and research projects. These activities are designed to document, assess and confirm or disprove the hypothesis presented in the Communication from the Commission to the Council and Parliament on the integrated management of coastal zones (COM 511/95)(22) that the continued degradation and mismanagement of many of Europe's coastal areas can be traced to problems related to insufficient coordination between different levels and sectors of administration, insufficient participation and consultation of all of the relevant actors and insufficient or inappropriate information, both about the state of the coastal zones and also about the impact of human activities (economic and non‐economic).

As well as providing concrete examples of good practice in ICZM in a range of socio‐economic, cultural, administrative and physical conditions, the Demonstration Programme is also intended to be the raw material for proposing the development of a European Strategy for ICZM.

A document has been compiled by the Programme's thematic experts presenting the conclusions of the programme to date and launching the question about the appropriate contents and form for a future European Strategy on ICZM, consistent with the principles of subsidiarity and proportionality. This document will be diffused in late April with comments welcomed until the end of September, following which the Commission will prepare its final recommendations.

Further information on the programme can be found on the Web page europa.eu.int/comm/dg11/iczm/home.htm.

Furthermore, the Commission has identified for the period 1999‐2002 the monitoring and managing of coastal processes and the coastal zone as a specific topic in the Fifth RTD Framework Programme (item 3.3 of the Programme ‘Energy, Environment and Sustainable Development', key action ‘Sustainable Marine Ecosystems'). In the first year, projects will concentrate on integrated studies on land‐ocean interaction while for the second year coastal protection against flooding and erosion and coastal processes monitoring are prioritised topics. The call for proposals of 20 March 1999, not being restricted to a particular European area, gives a good opportunity to propose also research projects for the east and north‐east coast of Ireland.

Further information on the current RTD activities can be found on the Web page http: //europa.eu.int/comm/dg12/eloise/eloise‐h.htm1 .

Question No 89 by Niall Andrews (H−0298/99)

Subject: Europol help‐line

Once Europol is fully operational, does the Commission consider that, in the context of fighting transnational organised crime, it would be possible or advisable for an EU‐wide free phone line to be set up to enable EU citizens to contact Europol in order to pass on information that might help to tackle organised crime, drugs and trafficking in human beings?

Answer

The Commission does not consider that it would be advisable to set up a Community‐wide free‐phone to enable citizens to contact Europol to pass on information that could help to tackle organised crime.

The objective of Europol is to improve the effectiveness and cooperation of the competent authorities in the Member States in preventing and combating terrorism, unlawful drug trafficking and other serious forms of international crime where there are factual indications that an organised criminal structure is involved and two or more Member States are affected by the forms of crimes in question in such a way as to require a common approach by the Member States owing to the scale, significance and consequences of the offences concerned.

In this context, it is still the task of the authorities in the Member States to evaluate information and intelligence in accordance with national law. It is then the task of the national units within the Member States to supply Europol on their own initiative with the information and intelligence necessary for it to carry out its task.

Question No 90 by Pat Gallagher (H−0300/99)

Subject: Dumping of munitions in the Irish Sea

The Commission will recall my question tabled in 1995 (H−0696/95)(23) on the extensive dumping by the UK of munitions, including chemical weapons, in the Irish Sea and off the north‐west coast of Ireland over a period of years (1920‐1945).

Can the Commission now indicate what it believes the position to be as regards the dumping of these munitions and chemical weapons and to what extent it has pursued this issue since I first raised it in 1995?

Answer

The Commission recognizes, as Mrs Bjerregaard stated on 10 October 1995 in reply to Oral Question H−696/95(24) , that there is a potential risk posed by munitions including chemical weapons dumped in the Irish Sea and off the North West coast of Ireland in the past.

This issue is however more appropriately addressed by the Member States concerned in the context of the OSPAR Commission. OSPAR, the body responsible for the Convention for the Protection of the Marine Environment of the North East Atlantic, last year agreed strategies aimed at progressively limiting the input of hazardous substances to and ionizing radiation in the marine environment.

While these strategies are basically aimed at preventing future activities harmful to the marine environment, OSPAR will, based on a document prepared by Ireland, discuss possible action on this issue at its next Heads of Delegation meeting in May.

The Commission is of the understanding that the Irish document will contain updated information on munitions dumped in the past and more recent events such as the washing up on beaches of weapons which have ‘escaped' from the dumping sites.

Question No 91 by Liam Hyland (H−0302/99)

Subject: Prospects for the future rural policy

A quarter of the population of the European Union lives in rural areas. Looking to the future and given the need to ensure that the number of people living in rural areas throughout the European Union does not decrease any further, will the outgoing Commission indicate what it considers its legacy to be in relation to rural policy, how it views progress on the conclusions of the Cork Declaration, and whether it believes that the legacy of the Berlin Summit will be the preservation of rural life, jobs, culture and a unique European heritage?

Answer

The Agricultural Council compromise and the Berlin Council Conclusions put into place the framework for rural development proposed by the Commission, that is, to establish rural development policy as a second pillar of the CAP.

We should not forget that previous reforms of the Common Agricultural Policy and the Structural Funds created a patchwork of regulations and a territorially fragmented approach to rural development. Local and regional actors and rural communities were unable to fully play their role.

The current reform establishes a new framework for rural development which is built on the Cork Declaration. We have made programming, which now covers all rural areas, both simpler and more flexible and, indeed, compatible with the principles of sustainable development. It will tap into the potential of people and agencies living, working or operating in rural areas, and be reinforced by a Community initiative for local rural development. Above all, we now have an instrument which has the potential to accompany further change in the Common Agricultural Policy.

The European Council has endorsed this framework as the most effective way of preserving rural life, jobs, culture and the unique European heritage. It is to be hoped that the financial resources available are enough to fully reap the benefits of this new approach. It is disappointing that suggestions by certain Member States to increase the budget available for rural development disappeared in the final agreement in Berlin. However, the new arrangements for rural development will be more flexible and would enable the budgetary authorities to rebalance expenditure were it thought appropriate.

Question No 92 by Brian Crowley (H−0303/99)

Subject: The European Commission and ‘hacker‐safe' systems

Is the Commission satisfied that its computer systems and those of all the European institutions are ‘hacker‐safe', and has it taken steps to ensure that illegal access to its databases is impossible?

Answer

The Commission has made stringent efforts over recent years to improve the security of its computer networks and computer systems, and these efforts continue. The Commission cannot answer for the internal measures of other European institutions.

Question No 93 by Gerardo Galeote Quecedo (H−0311/99)

Subject: Commission communication on a new association between the European Union and Latin America for the XXIst Century

The Commission recently published a communication on a new association between the European Union and Latin America for the XXIst Century which coincides with the forthcoming European Union‐Latin America and Caribbean Summit to be held in June in Rio.

In view of the importance of relations between the Union and Latin America:

How does the Commission intend to carry out the proposals contained in its communication, and in what time frame?

Does the Commission consider that a global action programme between the Union and Latin America can be developed in the near future?

What is the Commission's opinion of the proposals addressed at the European Union‐Latin America and Caribbean Summit contained in the Final Act of the XIVth Interparliamentary Conference?

Answer

The aim of the Commission communication to which the honourable Member refers is to contribute to the preparations for the coming summit of the Heads of State and Government in Rio. Naturally, the Commission hopes that as many of the document"s proposals as possible will be accepted by the Heads of State and Government and thus reflected in the final texts adopted in Rio. It is hoped, in particular, that the Heads of State and Government will reach agreement on a priority programme of joint activities that would form the point of reference for cooperation in the years ahead. Clearly, the Commission and – at a bilateral level – the Member States will then have to translate the Rio conclusions into practical measures.

The Commission believes that this approach answers the honourable Member"s concern about the development of a new global action programme for Latin America.

The Commission has also taken due note of the message from the XIVth Interparliamentary Conference to the Heads of State and Government of Europe, Latin America and the Caribbean. Moreover, it is aware that there is substantial agreement between honourable Members and the Commission on two fundamental points in particular:

‐ firstly, the need to consolidate the central role of bodies promoting regional integration in dialogue with the EU;

‐ secondly, the need to allow bodies representing civil society to play a greater part in this dialogue than they have done in the past.

Question No 94 by Robert Evans (H−0312/99)

Subject: Non‐EU countries joining the single currency

In view of the fact that negotiations have taken place to allow three non‐EU countries, Monaco, San Marino and the Vatican City to join the single currency, can the Commission clarify the criteria for membership?

Are there other non‐EU states in the process of joining and, if so, is this not a completely anomalous situation and one that should be stopped?

Answer

On 31 December 1998 the Council took three decisions authorising the conclusion of bilateral agreements on monetary issues and exchange rates between the European Union and the Principality of Monaco, the Republic of San Marino and the Vatican City.

These decisions authorise France to conduct the negotiations with the Principality of Monaco and Italy to conduct those with the Republic of San Marino and the Vatican City. The Council decisions lay down the position to be adopted in the negotiations.

France and Italy have long been linked to the Principality of Monaco, in the case of the former, and the Republic of San Marino and the Vatican City, in the case of the latter, by agreements on monetary issues and exchange rates. These agreements authorise the third countries in question to issue coins denominated, as appropriate, in French francs or Italian lire and stipulate that notes and coins issued by France or Italy are legal tender on the territory of the third countries in question.

In adopting these decisions, the Council has honoured the commitment stemming from Declaration No 6 annexed to the Treaty on European Union, in which the Community undertakes to facilitate the renegotiation of existing arrangements with the Principality of Monaco, the Republic of San Marino and the Vatican City following the introduction of the single currency.

Question No 95 by Markus Ferber (H−0313/99)

Subject: Air quality in the buildings of the European Patent Office

Members of staff of the European Patent Office have been complaining for years of health problems caused by the air conditioning system and by noxious substances in offices. Following official complaints in this regard by several members of staff, the Patent Office commissioned a number of evaluations, the results of which were for the most part not made publicly available.

What measures does the Commission intend to take to induce the European Patent Office to make the results of the investigations on germ contamination available to the offices and persons concerned?

Can the Commission urge the European Patent Office to say whether the air conditioning system has in the past been cleaned using chemicals and, if so, which?

What does the Commission intend to do to ensure that the Patent Office carry out a standardized poll of all people working in its offices in order to shed light on the issue of ‘sick building syndrome'?

Does the Commission intend to take the necessary measures to establish a legal framework for the procedures dealt with by the invalidity assessment committees?

Answer

International health and safety legislation must be complied with by the agencies and by all the other institutions.

However, the Commission has no authority in this area with regard to the agencies as they are independent bodies. The Commission is therefore unable to reply to the honourable Member"s question.

Question No 96 by Susan Waddington (H−0314/99)

Subject: Discrimination at Border Controls

In its unsatisfactory response to my written questions, (P−2892/98, E−3769/98), the Commission stated that it is unaware of any problems of discrimination or racism against women during passport and customs checks, and therefore does not see a need to act. Such an answer belies the true state of affairs. As we have frequently seen over recent months, denying a problem's existence does not make it disappear. Given that the greatest weapon against discrimination is education, and in view of forthcoming Treaty changes, will the Commission now allocate resources to fund a study of the problem and measures designed to support the education of the officers who conduct these checks?

Question No 97 by Christine Oddy (H−0317/99)

Subject: Passport controls and fight against racism

What steps are the European Commissioners taking to ensure that European Union borders internally are not being patrolled in a racist way which penalises black European Union citizens?

Will the European Commission ensure that this is a priority after Article 13 of the Amsterdam Treaty comes into effect?

Joint answer

Border controls should not go beyond what is allowed by current Community legislation. Pursuant to this legislation, Member States must allow citizens of the Union to enter their territory simply on production of a valid identity card or passport subject only to the limitations justified on grounds of public policy, public security or public health. Members of the family who are nationals of a country for which a visa is required must produce a visa and Member States shall accord to such persons every facility for obtaining them.

The Commission shares the view of the honourable MEPs that border controls should not give rise to racial discriminations.

Racist controls constitute an infringement of the fundamental law of every Member State. Moreover when they are carried out on the occasion of passing through the frontier, they are also contrary to the fundamental right to free movement for persons established in Article 8a of the EC Treaty. Above all, it is incumbent on the authorities of each Member State to ensure that their officials, in carrying out their duties, respect these fundamental rights, which implies a particular vigilance on the part of the Member States.

Nevertheless, it is worth noting that on 11 and 12 December 1998 the Vienna European Council underlined the necessity to fight against all forms of racism, both in the Union and in third countries. In this respect, the Commission was invited to present proposals to the forthcoming Cologne Council, which would cover applicant countries as well as Member States. Some such initiatives have already been undertaken in the framework of the PHARE programme.

As regards Article 13 of the Treaty of Amsterdam, the Commission will analyse the possibility of proposing measures aimed at combating all forms of discrimination in the Union, including discrimination based on race or ethnic origin, once the new Treaty has entered into force.

However, in accordance with its statement of 17 March 1999, the Commission had decided not to take fresh political initiatives.
